Ashraf al-Nu'ut Sharh Musallam al-Thubut. 188. And being good, and then the very next day, the day of 'Eid, its being prohibited and evil is a puzzle that can only be known from the Shari'ah; reason can never reach it. When the pure Shari'ah was revealed, the Shari'ah made clear that the fast of the last day of Ramadan is good in itself, so it was made obligatory, and the fast of the first day of Shawwal is evil in itself, so it was made prohibited. Because the Wise Lawgiver does not command anything until He has deposited in it that capacity. When He commanded the fast of the last day of Ramadan, it was known that it contains the capacity for reward, and when He forbade the fast of 'Eid, it was known that it contains the capacity for punishment. At this point, the author has annotated under his words 'but the Shari'ah reveals.' It is this: 'And it is not hidden that this is bigotry, rather reason judges that there is no difference except by the Shari'ah. The utmost that can be said is that the obligation to subdue the soul is simply fasting in absolute terms, and the designation of the month of Ramadan is due to its virtue like the revelation of the Quran and other than that. So it follows that the first of Shawwal is the end of fasting, and the end of a thing is outside of it in terms of ruling. So ponder deeply.' The author in his above annotation levels a charge against the Mu'tazila: that attributing to reason this type of good and evil, which can only be known from the Shari'ah, is based on bigotry and narrow-mindedness, even though they also said that the Shari'ah has manifested its intrinsic good and evil. Whereas this type of good and evil is purely legal; reason has absolutely no way to it. Because when reason looks at the last day of Ramadan and the day of 'Eid, and at the fast of the last day of Ramadan and the fast of 'Eid, it finds both days and both fasts identical in essence. Therefore, according to reason, a ruling of good for one day and its fast, and a ruling of evil for the other day and its fast, is not valid; otherwise, preference without a preponderant would be necessary. So it must be admitted that the goodness and evil of both days and both fasts is purely legal, not rational. Yes, at most it can be said here that goodness, according to reason, is the subjugation and overpowering of the soul, and this is achieved by absolute fasting. Then this fast was attached to Ramadan because... Scanned by CamScanner. 189. ...the month of Ramadan has additional virtue due to the revelation of the Quran and other things, and this is the principle: when a ruling is restricted and specified to a thing, then beyond that thing the ruling is absent and cut off. So according to this principle, the first of Shawwal is beyond Ramadan, therefore fasting does not remain good in it; rather, fasting in it is evil accidentally because it is turning away from the feast of Allah. In contrast, the last of Ramadan, since it falls within Ramadan, fasting in it is good. In short, the goodness of the fast of the last day of Ramadan and the evil of the fast of the day of 'Eid, even if reason knows them, it is through accidental designation, not intrinsic. Rather, this accidental perception also comes to reason only because the Shari'ah reveals it, so it is not correct to call it rational. 12. Text of al-Muslim: 'Then they differed: the early ones said (good and evil) are due to the essence of the act; the later ones said rather due to a real attribute that necessitates them in both; and a group (said) a real attribute only in evil, and good is the absence of evil. And the Jubbaiyyah said it is not a real attribute but rather considerations. And the truth according to us is absolute generality, so abrogation does not apply to us.' Translation: Then the Mu'tazila differed. The early Mu'tazila said (the goodness and evil of an act) is due to the essence of the act; the later Mu'tazila said it is due to a real attribute that causes goodness and evil in the good and evil act; and a group of Mu'tazila said that in evil there is only a real attribute causing evil, and as for good, the absence of that attribute of evil suffices. And the Jubbaiyyah said it is not a real attribute but rather considerations. And the truth according to us is absolute generality, so the objection of abrogation does not apply to us. Elucidation: When the author finished mentioning the agreed-upon collective opinion of the Mu'tazila sects that the goodness and evil of acts are intrinsic and rational and necessitate a ruling, he intended to express their differing opinion, so he said 'Then they differed,' etc. Note that among the Mu'tazila there is intense disagreement on what is the cause and reason for the goodness and evil in an act: whether goodness and evil are in the reality of the act itself or due to an additional matter, and then whether good and evil have the same ruling or separate, and if due to an additional matter, whether it is real or relational. Scanned by CamScanner
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Mu'tazila doctrine of husn (goodness) and qubh (evil) and their disagreement, followed by the Hanafi position. The author begins by using the example of fasting on the last day of Ramadan (obligatory) and the first day of Shawwal (prohibited) to illustrate that the goodness or evil of an act is purely determined by the Shari'ah, not reason. Reason sees both days and fasts as identical, so any distinction must come from divine revelation. The author critiques the Mu'tazila for claiming that such rulings are rational, arguing that this is bigotry. He then presents a matn from 'Musallam al-Thubut' outlining the internal Mu'tazila dispute: the early Mu'tazila (Qadimun) held that goodness and evil are inherent in the essence of the act; the later ones (Mutā'khkhirun) said they are due to a real attribute that causes both; a third group said only evil has a real attribute, while good is merely the absence of that attribute; and the Jubbā'iyya said they are not real attributes but relational considerations. The Hanafi truth is 'al-itlaq al-a'mm' (absolute generality), meaning that acts are not inherently good or evil but receive their legal value from the Shari'ah. This refutes the Mu'tazila claim that abrogation (naskh) would be impossible if acts had intrinsic moral value, because if a ruling changes, the act's inherent value would contradict. The Hanafi position avoids this problem because acts are considered neutral until the Shari'ah assigns a ruling.

Key Terms
حسن و قبح (husn wa qubh): Goodness and evil of acts; in this passage, the subject of dispute between Hanafis and Mu'tazila on whether they are intrinsic/rational or purely legal.
الاطلاق الاعم (al-itlaq al-a'mm): Absolute generality; the Hanafi position that acts have no inherent moral value; their legal status depends entirely on divine command.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position explained in this passage, why is the fast of the last day of Ramadan good and the fast of the first day of Shawwal evil?
A) Because reason distinguishes them.
B) Because the Shari'ah has declared them so.
C) Because they have intrinsic different attributes.
D) Because of the virtue of Ramadan month.

Key Takeaway
The example of fasting on Ramadan and Shawwal proves that husn and qubh are not intrinsic but are assigned by divine command.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
The Mu'tazila Internal Dispute and the Hanafi Principle of al-Itlaq al-A'mm
Exposition of Mu'tazila internal differences and Hanafi final position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four Mu'tazila views on the nature of husn and qubh and state the Hanafi position of al-itlaq al-a'mm and its implication for abrogation.

Core Idea
Only the Hanafi principle of absolute generality avoids the problem of abrogation.

After presenting the Hanafi critique, the text turns to the internal disagreement among the Mu'tazila themselves. They differed on whether goodness and evil are due to essence, real attributes, or mere considerations. The author then declares the Hanafi truth: al-itlaq al-a'mm, which holds that acts are entirely neutral until the Shari'ah rules. This principle elegantly resolves the issue of naskh (abrogation) that troubled the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four Mu'tazila positions
List the views: 1) early (Qadimun): due to essence; 2) later (Mutā'khkhirun): due to a real attribute that causes both; 3) some: attribute only in evil, good is absence; 4) Jubbā'iyya: not real but considerations.
These are all metaphysical attempts to locate moral value.
2
Understand the Hanafi response
The response is al-itlaq al-a'mm: acts have no inherent value at all. This bypasses the entire Mu'tazila debate.
It is a claim about the nature of moral predication.
3
Derive the implication for naskh
Because acts are neutral, abrogation does not cause a contradiction; the ruling simply changes. The Mu'tazila struggle to explain how an intrinsically good act could become evil.
This is presented as an advantage of the Hanafi view.
4
Evaluate the strength
The Hanafi position is consistent and avoids metaphysical complications. It also aligns with the Qur'anic principle that rulings are by divine decree.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Mu'tazila doctrine of husn (goodness) and qubh (evil) and their disagreement, followed by the Hanafi position. The author begins by using the example of fasting on the last day of Ramadan (obligatory) and the first day of Shawwal (prohibited) to illustrate that the goodness or evil of an act is purely determined by the Shari'ah, not reason. Reason sees both days and fasts as identical, so any distinction must come from divine revelation. The author critiques the Mu'tazila for claiming that such rulings are rational, arguing that this is bigotry. He then presents a matn from 'Musallam al-Thubut' outlining the internal Mu'tazila dispute: the early Mu'tazila (Qadimun) held that goodness and evil are inherent in the essence of the act; the later ones (Mutā'khkhirun) said they are due to a real attribute that causes both; a third group said only evil has a real attribute, while good is merely the absence of that attribute; and the Jubbā'iyya said they are not real attributes but relational considerations. The Hanafi truth is 'al-itlaq al-a'mm' (absolute generality), meaning that acts are not inherently good or evil but receive their legal value from the Shari'ah. This refutes the Mu'tazila claim that abrogation (naskh) would be impossible if acts had intrinsic moral value, because if a ruling changes, the act's inherent value would contradict. The Hanafi position avoids this problem because acts are considered neutral until the Shari'ah assigns a ruling.

Key Terms
الاطلاق الاعم (al-itlaq al-a'mm): Absolute generality; the Hanafi position that acts have no inherent moral value; their legal status depends entirely on divine command.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation; the replacement of a legal ruling by a later ruling. The Hanafi position on itlaq allows naskh without contradiction because acts have no fixed intrinsic value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi position that resolves the issue of abrogation (naskh)?
A) Acts are intrinsically good or evil.
B) Acts have a real attribute that causes good and evil.
C) Acts are neither good nor evil until the Shari'ah rules.
D) Acts are good by nature but can become evil by human convention.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of al-itlaq al-a'mm declares acts neutral, solving the abrogation problem that plagues Mu'tazila theories.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
Four Mu'tazila Views on Husn and Qubh and the Hanafi/Maturidi Resolution
Exposition of four Mu'tazila positions on husn/qubh and the Maturidi response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four Mu'tazila positions on the nature of husn and qubh, identify the Hanafi/Maturidi position of 'itlaq a'mm', and explain how this position preserves the possibility of naskh.

Core Idea
Husn and qubh are not real attributes but Shariah-based considerations, allowing abrogation.

The Mu'tazila were not a single bloc on whether actions are inherently good or bad. This passage from Ashraf al-Nu'ut clarifies four distinct views within the Mu'tazila and then presents the Hanafi/Maturidi position that avoids a major objection: the impossibility of abrogation. Understanding this debate is key to appreciating the Hanafi usul principle that rulings are based on divine command, not intrinsic moral qualities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four Mu'tazila groups
Locate the four groups mentioned in the text: Qudama, Muta'akhkhirun, the group of Abu al-Hasan al-Basri, and the Juba'iyyah. Note their distinct views on the basis of husn and qubh.
The text uses 'Qudama', 'Muta'akhkhirun', 'Qawm', and 'Juba'iyyah'.
2
Distinguish each view's ontological claim
For each group, determine whether they hold husn/qubh to be essential (dhati), real attributes (sifa haqiqiyya), or mere considerations (i'tibar). Note that the third group distinguishes between husn and qubh.
Early Mu'tazila (Qudama): essential. Later Mu'tazila: real attribute in both. Al-Basri: real attribute only in qubh. Juba'iyyah: mere considerations.
3
Appreciate the common Mu'tazila premise
All four groups affirm that reason can independently know husn and qubh—this is the shared Mu'tazila stance. Their disagreement is only about the ontological basis of that moral quality.
This rationalist premise is what the Hanafi/Maturidi school rejects.
4
Learn the Hanafi/Maturidi objection
The text states that if husn/qubh were real attributes, naskh (abrogation) would be impossible because an act that is inherently good could never become bad. Since naskh is a reality in Shariah, the attributes cannot be real.
This is a rational (dalil aqli) argument.
5
State the Hanafi position and its consequence
The Maturidi/Hanafi position is 'itlaq a'mm' (absolute universal): husn and qubh are relational considerations (i'tibar) determined by Shariah. This means the ruling can change with abrogation, and reason alone cannot judge moral value without revelation.
This position avoids the naskh objection and aligns with the Ash'ari view that moral value is command-based.

ملحق کیا گیا کہ شہر رمضان کو نزول قرآن وغیرہ کے سبب مزید فضیلت حاصل ہے اور یہ ضابطہ ہے کہ حکم کو جب کی شئی کے ساتھ مقدر و مخصوص کر دیا جائے تو اس شی کے ماوراء میں حکم معدوم و منقطع ہوتا ہے، پس اس ضابطہ کے بموجب اول شوال ماوراء رمضان ہے اس لئے روزہ اس میں حسن نہ رہے گا بلکہ اس میں روزہ فتیح بالعرض ہوگا کہ ضیافتہ اللہ سے اعراض ہے، اسکے برخلاف آخری رمضان چونکہ رمضان میں داخل ہے اس لئے روزہ اس میں حسن ہو گا، غرض یہ کہ آخری رمضان کے روزہ کا حسن اور عید کے دن کے روزہ کا فتح اگر عقل کو معلوم بھی ہوا ہے تو وہ تقدیر عرضی سے ہوا ہے ذاتی سے نہیں۔ بلکہ یہ ادراک

(۲) متاخرین معتزلہ کے مذہب کا بیان ہے وہ کہتے ہیں کہ حسن و فتح ذات فعل یا نفس فعل کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے بلکہ فعل میں ایک ایسی صفت حقیقیہ غیر اعتبار یہ پائی جاتی ہے جو ذات فعل پر زائد ہوتی ہے وہی صفت حسن و قبح کی مقتضی ہوتی ہے۔ یادر ہے کہ متاخرین معتزلہ کا مذہب، مذہب اشاعرہ کے ساتھ قدرے مشترک ہے کہ وہ اسبات میں اشاعرہ کے موافق ہیں کہ فعل کا حسن و فتح ذات فعل کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے البتہ دونوں مذہب کے درمیان فرق اس میں ہے کہ معتزلہ کے نزدیک فعل میں ایک صفت حقیقیہ پائی جاتی ہے جو فعل میں حسن و قبح کے وجود کا موجب ہوتی ہے غرض موجب حسن و فتح ان کے نزدیک صفت ہی ہے اور جبکہ اشاعرہ کے نزدیک موجب شارع فاعل ہے۔
قوله وقوم لصفة حقيقية فى القبح فقط والحسن الخ
(۳) ابوالحسن بصری معتزلی اور اس کے متبعین کے مذہب کو ذکر کیا ہے۔ ان کا خیال ہے کہ حسن و قبح دونوں صفتیں فصل کی ، صفت حقیقیہ کی وجہ سے نہیں ہیں بلکہ صرف صفت فتح فعل میں صفت حقیقیہ کی وجہ سے ثابت ہے جو تیج میں فتح کو لازم کرتی ہے، رہا حسن تو اس میں کسی صفت حقیقیہ زائدہ کی ضرورت نہیں ہے بلکہ اس میں موجب فتح کا نہ ہونا جس کے لئے کافی ہے۔
قوله - وقال الجبائية ليست صفة حقيقية
(۴) ابوعلی محمد بن عبد الوھاب جہائی اور اس کے قبعین:۔ کے مذہب کا بیان ہے ان کا نظریہ ہے کہ حسن و قبح ذات فعل کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے بلکہ ایسی صفت کی وجہ سے ہے جو ان دونوں میں حسن و فتح کہ و کی
Attached: the month of Ramadan, due to the revelation of the Quran etc., has additional virtue. And the rule is: when a ruling is designated and restricted to a thing, then the ruling is absent and discontinued beyond that thing. Therefore, by this rule, the first of Shawwal is beyond Ramadan, so fasting on it will not be good; rather, fasting on it will be bad by accident, because it is turning away from God's hospitality. In contrast, the last of Ramadan, since it is within Ramadan, fasting on it will be good. In short, even if the intellect knows the goodness of the last day of Ramadan and the badness of the day of Eid, that knowledge comes by accidental appreciation, not essential. Rather, this accidental perception is also attained by reason only through the disclosure of the Shariah, so calling it rational is not correct. 12. Text of al-Muslim: Then they differed. The ancients said: due to the essence of the act; and the later ones said: rather, due to a real attribute that necessitates it in both; and a group said: due to a real attribute only in badness, and goodness is the absence of badness; and the Juba'iyyah said: it is not a real attribute but considerations. And the truth in our view is the absolute universal, so abrogation is not objected against us. Translation: Then among the Mu'tazila there was disagreement. The early Mu'tazila said: the goodness and badness of acts are due to the essence of the act. The later Mu'tazila said: due to a real attribute that necessitates goodness and badness in good and bad acts. A group of Mu'tazila said: in badness, it is due to a real attribute, and as for goodness, the absence of the attribute of badness is sufficient. And the Juba'iyyah said: it is not a real attribute but considerations. And the truth in our view is the absolute universal, so abrogation is not objected against us. Explanation: When the author finished mentioning the unanimous and consensus view of the Mu'tazila sects that the goodness and badness of acts are essential and rational, and that they necessitate ruling, he intended to mention their conflicting views, so he said "then they differed," etc. Note that among the Mu'tazila there is intense disagreement about what is the cause and reason of goodness and badness in an act: whether goodness and badness are in the essence of the act itself or due to an additional matter, and whether both goodness and badness have the same ruling or different. And if due to an additional matter, is it real or considered?

To explain this detail, the author mentioned the four Mu'tazila sects and the Maturidi sect of Ahl al-Sunnah. His saying: "then they differed, the ancients said: due to the essence of the act" (1) This describes the view of the early Mu'tazila: they say that the goodness and badness of acts are not due to an external matter, and there is no additional attribute in the act that necessitates its goodness or badness; rather, goodness and badness are due to the essence of the act itself. His saying: "and the later ones said: rather, due to a real attribute that necessitates it in both" (2) This describes the view of the later Mu'tazila: they say that goodness and badness are not due to the essence or the act itself; rather, there is a real, non-considered attribute existing in the act that is additional to the essence, and that attribute is what necessitates goodness and badness. Note that the view of the later Mu'tazila shares some commonality with the Ash'ari school in that both deny that goodness and badness are due to the essence of the act. However, the difference between the two schools is that the Mu'tazila believe that a real attribute exists in the act that causes goodness and badness to exist in the act—in short, the cause of goodness and badness in their view is an attribute—while the Ash'ari hold that the cause is the Lawgiver's command. His saying: "and a group said: due to a real attribute only in badness, and goodness..." (3) This mentions the view of Abu al-Hasan al-Basri al-Mu'tazili and his followers. Their opinion is that goodness and badness are not both due to a real attribute; rather, only the attribute of badness in an act is due to a real attribute that necessitates badness in the act. As for goodness, no additional real attribute is needed; rather, the absence of the cause of badness is sufficient for it. His saying: "and the Juba'iyyah said: it is not a real attribute" (4) This describes the view of Abu Ali Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab al-Juba'i and his followers. Their theory is that goodness and badness are not due to the essence of the act, but due to an attribute that is real...
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the Mu'tazila positions on the nature of husn (goodness) and qubh (badness) in actions, followed by the Maturidi (Hanafi) position. The author first sets up the context: the earlier discussed unanimous Mu'tazila view that acts inherently possess goodness or badness known by reason. Then he presents the four divergent views within Mu'tazila. The first view (Qudama) holds that husn and qubh are essential to the act itself—they are not due to an external or additional attribute. The second view (Muta'akhkhirun) says there is a real attribute (sifa haqiqiyya) in the act that causes husn and qubh; this is close to the Ash'ari view except that the Mu'tazila attribute causation to the attribute while Ash'aris attribute it to the Shari'. The third view (Abu al-Hasan al-Basri) holds that only qubh is due to a real attribute; husn is merely the absence of that attribute. The fourth view (Juba'i and his followers) denies that husn and qubh are real attributes at all; they are mere considerations (i'tibar). Finally, the Maturidi position is stated: 'al-itlaq al-a'mm' (the absolute universal), meaning that these attributes are not real but are relational considerations. This position avoids the problem of naskh (abrogation): if husn and qubh were real and necessary, then a command that was good could not later become bad, but since they are considered, abrogation is possible. The commentary further explains the nuance, noting that the Muta'akhkhirun view is partially shared by Ash'aris, but they differ on the cause. The passage thus clarifies the internal Mu'tazila diversity and defends the Hanafi/Maturidi stance.

Key Terms
حسن (husn): Goodness of an act; in this context, the quality that makes an act praiseworthy and deserving of reward according to reason (according to Mu'tazila) or according to Shariah (according to Hanafis).
قبح (qubh): Badness of an act; the quality that makes an act blameworthy and prohibited.
ذات الفعل (dhat al-fi'l): The essence of the act itself, without any additional attribute.
صفة حقيقية (sifa haqiqiyya): A real attribute that exists externally in the act, not merely a mental consideration.
اعتبار (i'tibar): A consideration or relational aspect, not a real attribute existing externally.
اطلاق اعم (itlaq a'mm): Absolute universal; the Hanafi/Maturidi view that the attribution of husn and qubh is general and not necessarily tied to real attributes, allowing for naskh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Mu'tazila group held that goodness (husn) is merely the absence of badness (qubh)?
A) The Qudama (ancients) who said husn and qubh are due to essence of act
B) The Muta'akhkhirun (later ones) who said both are due to a real attribute
C) The group of Abu al-Hasan al-Basri who said only qubh is due to a real attribute, and husn is its absence
D) The Juba'iyyah who said husn and qubh are considerations

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi/Maturidi position that husn and qubh are Shariah-determined considerations ('itlaq a'mm') resolves the naskh objection and affirms the primacy of revelation in moral judgment.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Five Positions on Husn wa Qubh: The Maturidi Doctrine of General Absoluteness
Comparative exposition of five theological positions on moral value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the five positions on the nature of husn and qubh (early Mutazila, later Mutazila, Abu al-Hasan al-Basri, Jubbaiyya, Maturidi), and explain why the Maturidi view avoids the naskh objection.

Core Idea
Moral value is not fixed to a single cause; the Maturidi 'general absoluteness' allows abrogation without contradiction.

This passage from Sharh Musallam al-Thubut maps out five competing theological views on what makes an action good or evil. The Maturidi (and thus Hanafi) position is presented as the truth because it alone avoids the logical problem of naskh (abrogation). Understanding this dispute is crucial for appreciating how the Hanafi school views the relationship between reason, revelation, and change in divine law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five positions
List the five views in order: (1) early Mutazila: husn/qubh due to essence; (2) later Mutazila: due to a real attribute; (3) Abu al-Hasan al-Basri: only evil due to real attribute, goodness is absence; (4) Jubbaiyya: due to a relational attribute; (5) Maturidi: general absoluteness, not restricted to any single cause.
The later Mutazila view partially agrees with Ash'arism in denying essence-based morality, but the cause is still an attribute not the Lawgiver.
2
Examine the Maturidi position in detail
The Maturidi 'al-itlaq al-a'amm' means husn/qubh can arise from essence, a real attribute, or a relational aspect. The key is flexibility: no single cause is universally necessary.
This avoids the problem of naskh because if morality were fixed to essence, a good act could never become bad, making abrogation impossible.
3
Understand the naskh objection
The problem: If an act is essentially good (early Mutazila) or has a necessary real attribute making it good (later Mutazila), then it can never become evil. But divine law abrogates previous rulings, implying change in moral status. This would be impossible if morality is fixed to essence or a necessary attribute.
The Maturidi response: since morality is not pinned to a single cause, naskh is unproblematic.
4
Analyze the practical thamarah al-khilaf
The dispute has real consequences for usul al-fiqh: can a ruling be abrogated without changing the nature of the act? For Maturidis, yes; for most Mutazila, no, which leads to difficulties with established cases of naskh (e.g., change of qibla).
The Hanafi school follows the Maturidi position, upholding the possibility of naskh.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text declares the Maturidi view as al-haqq (the truth). The tarjih is based on its logical consistency: it accommodates naskh and the variety of moral intuitions across acts.
No explicit citation of a classical tarjih authority, but the reasoning is accepted by the Hanafi usuli tradition.

اعتبار سے مختلف ہو سکتی ہے چنانچہ حسن کبھی فتح ہو سکتا ہے اس طرح فتح کبھی حسن میں بدل سکتا ہے جیسے ضرب یتیم ایک ایسا فعل ہے جو باختلاف اعتبارات حسن و قبح دونوں ہے یتیم کو ادب دینے کے لئے مارنا اعتبار کسی خاص صفت یا خاص شق کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے بلکہ فعل میں حسن و فتح کا اطلاق شقوق سے عام ہے لہذا فعل کا حسن و قبیح نہ صرف ذات فعل کی وجہ سے ہے جیسا کہ متقدمین معتزلہ کا خیال ہے نہ صفت حقیقیہ کی وجہ سے ہے جیسا کہ متاخرین معتزلہ کا زعم ہے اور نہ ہی صفت زائدہ و اعتبارات کی وجہ سے ہے جیسا کہ بعض دوسرے معتزلیوں کا رجحان ہے۔ بلکہ ذات فعل ،صفت حقیقیہ، صفت زائدہ اور وجوہ واعتبارات جیسی قیدوں سے آزاد ہے اور سب کو عام ہے اس لئے ایسا ہو سکتا ہے کہ بعض فعل میں حسن و فتح ذات فعل کی وجہ سے ہو۔ بعض میں صفت حقیقیہ کی وجہ سے ہو جو اسکے حسن و فتح کی مقتضی ہو اور بعض میں صفت اعتباریہ کی وجہ سے ہو جیسے جہاد کا حسن اور قتل غیر مستحق کا فتح ، اول حسن ہے اس اعتبار سے کہ جہاد اعلاء کلمتہ اللہ کا سبب ہے اور ثانی قبیح ہے اس اعتبار سے کہ بنیان رب کو ناحق منہدم کرنا ہے الغرض حسن و قبح فعل کسی خاص قید و وجہ سےمخصوص ومقید نہیں بلکہ مطلق واعم ہے۔ قوله فلايرد النسخ علينا یہ عبارت مذہب اہلسنت ماتریدیہ پر متفرع ہے یا مذہب ماتریدیہ کی تائید کے لئے پیش کی ہے۔ مطلب یہ ہے کہ حسن و فتح کے بارے میں اطلاق عام کا قول کرنے سے ہم معاشر ماتریدیہ پر سخ سے اعتراض نہ پڑے گا مگر ہمارے برخلاف معتزلہ کے اقوال پر نسخ سے اعتراض پڑے گا۔ اعتراض کی تقریر :۔ یہ ہے کہ فعل کا حسن و فتح اگر ذات فعل، یا صفت حقیقیہ لازمہ کی وجہ سے ہو جیسا کہ معتزلہ کہتے ہیں تو نسخ کا بطلان لازم آئے گاوہ اسطرح کہ ذات کا مقتضیٰ کبھی ذات سے متخلف نہیں ہوتا، پس جب حسن و قبح بر بنائے مذہب معتزلہ ذات فعل کا مقتضی ہے تو ظاہر ہے کہ ذات فعل سے کبھی
Then he said: They disagreed. The predecessors said: due to the essence of the action. (1) This is the statement of the early Mutazila: they say that the goodness and evil of actions are not due to an external matter, rather goodness and evil are solely due to the essence of the act. Then he said: The later ones said: rather, due to a real attribute that necessitates it in both. (2) This is the statement of the later Mutazila: they say that goodness and evil are not due to the essence or the action itself, but rather there is a real, non-relational attribute in the act, superadded to its essence, and that attribute necessitates goodness and evil. Note that the later Mutazila's doctrine partially agrees with the Ash'aris in that both deny goodness/evil due to essence; however, the difference is that for Mutazila there is a real attribute in the act that causes goodness/evil, while for Ash'aris the cause is the Lawgiver. Then he said: And a people: due to a real attribute only in evil, and goodness... (3) This mentions the doctrine of Abu al-Hasan al-Basri al-Mu'tazili and his followers. They hold that goodness and evil are not both due to a real attribute; rather, only evil in the act is established by a real attribute that necessitates evil. As for goodness, no additional real attribute is needed; the absence of the cause of evil suffices. Then he said: And the Jabba'iyya said: it is not a real attribute. (4) This is the statement of Abu Ali Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab al-Jubbai and his followers. Their view is that goodness and evil are not due to the essence, but due to an attribute that necessitates goodness and evil, but that attribute is not real; rather it is relational, which can vary with different perspectives. Thus goodness can sometimes become evil, and evil can become goodness. For example, beating an orphan is an act that, varying by consideration, is both good and evil: beating to discipline is good, beating to harm is evil, even though the act of beating is one. Then he said: And the truth according to us is the most general absoluteness. (5) This explains the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna al-Maturidiyya. Its gist is that the goodness and evil of actions are not due to any specific attribute or specific category from those mentioned; rather, the attribution of goodness and evil to the action is general to all categories. Therefore, the goodness and evil of an act can be due to its essence (as the early Mutazila held), or due to a real attribute (as the later Mutazila alleged), or due to an added relational attribute (as some other Mutazila inclined). Rather, it is free from restrictions like essence, real attribute, added attribute, and perspectives; it encompasses all. So it is possible that in some acts goodness and evil are due to their essence, in others due to a real attribute that necessitates their goodness and evil, and in others due to a relational attribute, like the goodness of jihad and the evil of killing an undeserving person; the first is good because it is a means of elevating Allah's word, and the second is evil because it is wrongfully destroying a human being. In short, the goodness and evil of an act are not specific and restricted to a particular restriction or perspective, but are absolute and general. Then he said: So naskh does not apply objectionably to us. This sentence is a corollary of the Maturidi doctrine or presented to support it. The meaning is that by holding the general absoluteness regarding goodness and evil, we Maturidis will not face the objection of naskh, whereas the Mutazilite views do face that objection. The objection: If goodness and evil are due to the essence of the act or a necessary real attribute, as Mutazila say, then naskh would be invalid because the implication of the essence never departs from the essence; so when goodness and evil, on the Mutazilite doctrine, are implications of the essence, it is clear that they cannot be separated from the essence.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Musallam al-Thubut presents and critiques four Mutazilite positions on the nature of moral value (husn/qubh) before affirming the Maturidi position. The commentator first explains the early Mutazila view: goodness/evil are intrinsic to the act's essence itself, with no additional attribute. Then the later Mutazila: there is a real, ontologically extra attribute causing goodness/evil. A third group, led by Abu al-Hasan al-Basri, holds that only evil requires such an attribute; goodness is merely the absence of the cause of evil. The fourth group, the Jubbaiyya, says the attribute is relational (i'tibari), not real, hence goodness can become evil depending on consideration. The author then declares the Maturidi position: goodness and evil are not pinned to any single cause; they may arise from essence, real attribute, or relational aspect, depending on the act. This 'general absoluteness' (itlaq a'amm) avoids the objection of naskh. The objection is that if an act is essentially good, it can never become evil, so abrogation of a command would be impossible. But for Maturidis, since the valuation is not fixed to essence alone, a command can be abrogated (e.g., changing prayer direction), because the act's moral quality can shift due to other considerations.

Key Terms
حسن و قبح (husn wa qubh): Moral categories applied to actions: husn = good/beautiful/praiseworthy, qubh = evil/ugly/blameworthy. In usul al-fiqh, they relate to the legal value of acts.
الاطلاق الاعم (al-itlaq al-a'amm): The most general absoluteness: the attribution of husn/qubh to an action is unrestricted to any specific cause (essence, attribute, etc.).
صفت حقیقیہ (sifat haqiqiyya): A real, ontologically positive attribute in the action that necessitates its moral value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Jubbaiyya (Abu Ali al-Jubbai and followers), what is the nature of the attribute that causes goodness/evil in an act?
A) It is a real attribute inherent in the essence.
B) It is a relational (i'tibari) attribute that can vary with consideration.
C) Only evil is due to a real attribute; goodness requires none.
D) There is no attribute; goodness/evil are due to the essence itself.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi 'general absoluteness' (al-itlaq al-a'amm) holds that moral value arises from multiple possible causes, thereby avoiding the problem of naskh that plagues Mutazilite theories.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Reason as an Independent Source of Rulings: The Hanafi Internal Dispute
Clarifying the Hanafi internal dispute on reason's role in knowing rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three positions (majority Hanafis, some Hanafis, Mu'tazila) on whether reason independently entails rulings, and distinguish the limited scope of the some Hanafis from the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Some Hanafis hold reason independent only for iman and kufr; Mu'tazila extend it to all rulings.

In this passage from Sharh Musallam al-Thubut, the commentator clarifies an internal Hanafi dispute: do rational perceptions of good and evil (ḥusn wa qubḥ) automatically create legal obligations? While majority Hanafis say no, a group of prominent Hanafi scholars (including al-Maturidi) argues that reason is independent in a very limited set of rulings. Understanding the precise boundaries of this position is critical to avoid conflating it with the Mu'tazila, who grant reason far broader authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Read the passage to extract the views of: (1) majority Hanafis, (2) some Hanafis, and (3) Mu'tazila. Note their common ground (reason perceives good and evil) and point of disagreement (whether this entails a ruling).
The majority Hanafis treat ḥusn/qubḥ as ‘illa for preponderance, not obligation.
2
Understand the logical argument of majority Hanafis
The majority argue that Allah, being Wise, cannot issue a ruling without preponderance (tarjīḥ). Good and evil are not equal in His sight, but they do not automatically create wujūb or ḥurmah.
This is a dalīl ‘aqlī (rational proof).
3
Locate the some Hanafis' claim
Some Hanafis (al-Maturidi, Fakhr al-Islam, Sadr al-Sharia) assert that in a few matters—specifically faith (īmān) and disbelief (kufr)—reason independently knows the ruling. They base this on reason’s clear perception of the goodness of faith and evil of disbelief.
They do not extend this to all acts; only a very limited set.
4
Distinguish from the Mu'tazila
The Mu'tazila claim that reason is an independent source for many rulings, not just faith and disbelief. The passage emphasizes that the some Hanafis’ position is intermediate (bayn bayn) and not identical to the Mu'tazila.
The commentator criticizes those who conflate the two.
5
Apply to the mountaintop scenario
The passage concludes by connecting this dispute to the case of a person who reaches puberty alone on a mountaintop without revelation. According to the some Hanafis, such a person is obligated to believe by reason alone; the majority would withhold obligation until revelation reaches them.
This thamarah al-khilāf (practical consequence) illustrates the real-world impact.

For he sees from the evidences. I say: perhaps the meaning is after the passage of the period of reflection, for it is like the call of the messengers in awakening the heart, and that period varies because intellects differ. And by what we have written of the schools, the issue of the one who reaches puberty on a high mountain branches off. Translation: Then some Hanafi scholars have said that reason is independent in perceiving some of Allah's rulings. Accordingly, they declared faith obligatory even upon a sane child, and declared disbelief and everything not befitting Allah as forbidden. And it is narrated from Imam Abu Hanifa that no excuse will be accepted for ignorance of the Creator because the proofs for His existence are manifest. I think that perhaps after the period of reflection ends, no excuse is accepted, because the period of reflection is like the call of the messengers in awakening the heart, and it varies according to people because intellects differ. And from the schools we have recorded, the issue of the one who reaches puberty on a high mountain branches off. Explanation: It has been stated in previous pages that the Hanafis, despite agreeing with the Mu'tazila on the rationality of good and ugly, do not hold that they necessitate a ruling. Rather, the goodness or ugliness of an act is the cause (illa) for the preponderance of a ruling. So faith and disbelief cannot be equal in relation to Allah, as might be initially imagined, because He is such a Wise One that He cannot give a ruling for something without preponderance, nor prefer the preponderant. It is clear that the obligation of faith is preponderant and the prohibition of disbelief is preponderant, so they cannot be equal. However, good and ugly do not necessitate a ruling as the Mu'tazila believe they necessitate. But some Hanafis have differed from their group in that in some places the good (husn) entails a ruling. So the author intended to mention those some Hanafis who differ from all Hanafis in this issue. His statement: 'Then among the Hanafis are those who said that reason...' i.e., some Hanafi scholars like Imam al-Huda Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, Imam Fakhr al-Islam [author of al-Mizan], and Sadr al-Sharia, etc., have said that without the aid of revelation, reason can independently perceive some of Allah's rulings due to the goodness or ugliness of an act. Therefore, these eminent scholars declared faith obligatory upon every sane adult, even a sane child, whether the message of a messenger reached him or not, and declared disbelief and all things not befitting Allah like injustice and foolishness as forbidden, because reason has pointed out the goodness of faith and the ugliness of disbelief. In sum, according to these scholars, rulings are of two types: one in which reason is not independent, so reason cannot impose obligation or prohibition; the second is that in which reason is independent without aid of revelation, so reason can impose obligation or prohibition. The school of these some Hanafis lies between the school of the majority of Hanafis and the Mu'tazila: the majority claim that reason absolutely does not entail a ruling; the Mu'tazila claim that reason absolutely entails a ruling; and separate from both, the school of some Hanafis is that reason entails a ruling in some matters and not in others. From this presentation, it becomes clear that there is a distinct difference between the school of some Hanafis and the Mu'tazila. Therefore, the statement of some commentators of Musallam that there is no difference between some Hanafis and Mu'tazila, and then trying to answer, is a futile elongation. Indeed, I have before me three reliable Arabic commentaries of Musallam in which it is said that there is no difference in this issue between some Hanafis and Mu'tazila because the Mu'tazila claim that in some matters the ruling can be known by reason without dependence on revelation, and some Hanafis also say the same. However, a difference can be made in that the rulings in which some Hanafis consider reason independent are very few, like faith and disbelief, etc., whereas according to the Mu'tazila, the rulings known by reason are many. Some great scholars have made a distinction saying that according to the Mu'tazila they are and
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Musallam al-Thubut addresses an internal Hanafi dispute regarding the role of reason (aql) in deriving rulings. The commentator explains the positions of three groups: the majority of Hanafis (jumhur), some Hanafis (like al-Maturidi, Fakhr al-Islam, and Sadr al-Sharia), and the Mu'tazila. The majority Hanafis hold that reason does not necessitate any legal ruling; good and evil are rational attributes but do not themselves create obligation or prohibition. The Mu'tazila hold that reason fully necessitates rulings—whatever reason deems good or evil is accordingly obligatory or forbidden. The some Hanafis take a middle position: in limited matters, such as the obligation of iman and prohibition of kufr, reason can independently perceive the ruling without revelation. The commentator emphasizes that this position is distinct from the Mu'tazila, whose scope is much broader. He critiques those commentators of Musallam who conflate the two views, arguing that the difference is clear and that attempts to conflate them are futile. The passage also applies this dispute to a concrete case: one who reaches puberty alone on a mountaintop without access to revelation—does reason alone impose obligation?

Key Terms
العقل (al-aql): The rational faculty that can perceive the goodness and badness (husn wa qubh) of actions independently of revelation.
حسن وقبح (husn wa qubh): The moral qualities of actions: goodness (husn) and badness/badness (qubh) that reason can perceive.
مستلزم حکم (mustalzim hukm): Entailing a legal ruling; the idea that the goodness or badness of an action automatically generates a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between the position of 'some Hanafis' and the Mu'tazila regarding reason's ability to derive rulings?
A) The Mu'tazila hold that reason is never independent, while some Hanafis hold it is always independent.
B) Some Hanafis hold that reason is independent only in very few matters (like iman and kufr), while the Mu'tazila hold it is independent in many matters.
C) Both groups hold identical views with no difference whatsoever.
D) The Mu'tazila accept reason only after revelation, while some Hanafis reject reason entirely.

Key Takeaway
Some Hanafis allow reason to independently entail a ruling in very limited cases like iman and kufr, a middle position distinct from the majority Hanafis and the Mu'tazila.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Three Madhhabs on the Role of Intellect in Taklif
Dispute among three theological schools on aql's role in taklif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi/Maturidi position from Mu'tazila and Ash'ari regarding the role of aql in legal obligation, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Intellect neither causes nor prepares rulings—it necessitates obligation when given reflection time.

This passage from Sharh Musallam al-Thubut introduces a crucial usul al-fiqh dispute: does intellect (aql) directly produce legal rulings? The Mu'tazila say yes, the Ash'aris say no (it only prepares), and the Maturidi/Hanafi school takes a nuanced middle path. Understanding this difference clarifies when a person becomes accountable for belief.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three schools
List Mu'tazila, Ash'ari, and Maturidi/Hanafi as the three positions on aql's role.
2
Define each position on aql
Mu'tazila: aql is an effective cause (illa mujiba). Ash'ari: aql is a preparatory cause (illa muhayyi'a). Maturidi: aql is neither, it necessitates the ruling by making connection to the Lawgiver obligatory.
The passage uses 'علت موجبہ' and 'علت محمدرہ' (likely muhayyi'a).
3
Locate the Hanafi proof
The narration from Abu Hanifa (via Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) states that a rational person cannot be excused for ignorance of the Creator, implying aql's independent role.
Dalil block contains the text and analysis.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Mu'tazila would hold obligation from the moment of reason; Maturidi requires a period of reflection. This affects when punishment applies.

معنى قولنا يكلف بالعقل انه اذاعاته الله تعالى بالتجربة وامهله لدرک العواقب لم يكن معذوراً وان لم يبلغه الدعوة على نحو ماقال ابو حنيفة في السفيه انه اذا بلغ خمساً وعشرين سنة لا يمنع من ماله لانه قد استوفى مدة التجربة فلابد ان
يزداد رشداً وليس على الحد في هذا الباب دليل قاطع
In reality, all rulings are entailed, even though intellect perceives the beauty and ugliness of only some actions. The Maturidis have specified that intellect is independent in perceiving some rulings, but they did not say that it perceives all rulings from beauty and ugliness. However, some Hanafis will be accused of arbitrariness because they made intellect a governing [authority] for some actions and not for others. The essence of the dispute: from the words of Imam Fakhr al-Islam, it is understood that the dispute among Maturidis, Mu'tazila, and Ash'aris is that according to the Mu'tazila, intellect is an effective cause for the ruling; according to the Ash'aris, intellect is a preparatory cause; and according to the Maturidis, intellect is neither an effective cause nor a preparatory cause, rather intellect necessitates the ruling and [the ruling's] connection to the All-Knowing and All-Aware makes the ruling obligatory. His statement: 'and it is narrated from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, etc.' This phrase supports and confirms the stance of some Hanafis that from the narration of the great imam Abu Hanifa, it is apparent that intellect is independent in perceiving some rulings. Thus in the book Muntqala, then ... from Muhammad al-Shaybani, and in Jami' al-Asrar etc. from Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa, it is narrated that no rational person will be considered excused on the Day of Judgment for being ignorant of his Creator, but will be punished, because he observes the evidence and proofs of the existence of the Creator, His oneness, and His attributes of perfection—the creation of the heavens and earth, the alternation of night and day, other creatures and wonders of power, and his own creation—so there is no room for doubt in this. Establishing the knowledge of God rationally, here Fadil Khayrabadi said: The truth is that faith in Allah and His attributes is a perfection, and disbelief is a deficiency in the view of all rational people; also faith is gratitude for blessings, and gratitude is a perfection, and disbelief is ingratitude, which is a deficiency. So according to the rational person, faith is a quality of beauty and disbelief is a quality of ugliness. Therefore, it is necessary that intellect inclines toward beauty and expresses aversion to ugliness. Hence, a servant who abandons a rationally good act will be punished and not considered excused, even if no command has come from Allah. His statement: 'I say, perhaps the meaning is after the passage of the period of reflection, etc.' The above phrase is an answer to an objection. The formulation of the objection and answer is as follows. Objection: It is necessary from the statement of Imam Abu Hanifa and some Hanafis that faith in Allah and His attributes is obligatory without sending a messenger and conveying the message, so if a rational person dies without believing, he will be punished without the call of the messenger, because ignorance of his Creator is not an excuse, whereas Allah says: 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' (17:15). Answer: The summary of the answer is that the meaning of the statement of Imam Abu Hanifa etc. is not that mere attainment of reason is sufficient for faith in the Creator; rather, their intention is that after attaining reason, the person is given enough time and period in which he can reflect on the Creator, but the period of reflection passes and he dies without faith, so he will be punished even if the call of the messenger did not reach him, because the period of reflection is in the position of the call of the messenger. Just as the call of the messenger awakens the heart and informs the person about the existence of Allah, His oneness, and perfect attributes, similarly intellect in the period of reflection alerts the heart and mind. However, since humans are created with different temperaments and intellects, the intellect of each is not equal in terms of thinking, understanding, and reflection; rather, there is great variation among them, as hadiths, reports, observations, and experiences testify to the variation of intellects. Therefore, if the mentioned person did not find a period of reflection and died, he will not be punished because he did not attain the period of reflection which is in the position of the call of the messenger. The mentioned answer is derived from the principles of Fakhr al-Islam. Indeed, he said: The meaning of our statement 'he is obligated by intellect' is that when Allah gives him experience through trial and grants him time to comprehend consequences, he is not excused even if the call did not reach him. This is similar to what Abu Hanifa said about the foolish person: when he reaches twenty-five years of age, he is not prevented from his wealth, because he has completed the period of experience, so he must have increased in maturity. There is no decisive evidence on the limit in this matter.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Musallam al-Thubut (a Hanafi usul work) discusses the Maturidi position on the role of intellect (aql) in legal obligation (taklif) and the dispute with Mu'tazila and Ash'aris. The author first explains the three positions: Mu'tazila hold that aql is an effective cause (illa mujiba) for rulings; Ash'aris hold it is a preparatory cause (illa muhayyi'a); Maturidis hold it is neither but rather necessitates the ruling by making the connection to Allah necessary. Some Hanafis are criticized for inconsistency in granting aql authority only for some actions. The passage then cites a narration from Abu Hanifa (through Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) that a rational person cannot claim ignorance of the Creator because of the clear evidence in creation, implying that aql can independently know certain obligations like belief in God. Fadil Khayrabadi adds a rational argument: faith is a perfection and gratitude, disbelief is a deficiency and ingratitude; aql naturally inclines to perfection and beauty, so abandoning rationally good acts warrants punishment even without revelation. An objection is raised: this seems to contradict the Quranic verse 'We do not punish until We send a messenger' (17:15). The answer clarifies that the obligation is not based on mere aql alone, but on aql plus a sufficient period for reflection (muddah ta'ammul). This period serves as a substitute for the messenger's call. If a person has time to reflect but fails to believe, he is punishable; if he dies before having such time, he is not. This is based on Fakhr al-Islam's principle, analogized to Abu Hanifa's ruling that a foolish person reaching 25 years of age is deemed mature and cannot be prevented from his property—the period of experience suffices. Thus, the passage presents a nuanced Hanafi-Maturidi position balancing rational obligation with the condition of opportunity for reflection.

Key Terms
عقل (aql): Intellect or reason; the faculty by which humans perceive the goodness or evil of actions and may independently derive some rulings.
مدت تأمل (muddah ta'ammul): The period of reflection granted by Allah after attaining reason, in which a person can contemplate the existence and attributes of the Creator. This period stands in place of the messenger's call.
علت موجبہ (illa mujiba): Effective cause; in usul, a cause that directly produces its effect. Mu'tazila hold aql as such for rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi/Hanafi position, what is the role of intellect (aql) in legal obligation?
A) Aql is an effective cause that directly generates rulings.
B) Aql is a preparatory cause that prepares the ground for rulings.
C) Aql necessitates obligation only when a period of reflection is granted.
D) Aql has no role; only revelation matters.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi/Maturidi position on aql is that it necessitates obligation only when a period of reflection is granted, avoiding both the Mu'tazila extreme and the Ash'ari denial.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced

The Period of Reflection: Resolving the Objection to Rational Obligation
Resolving the conflict between rational obligation and the Quranic verse on sending messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Maturidi concept of muddah ta'ammul and apply it to reconcile rational obligation with Quran 17:15.

Core Idea
A period of reflection substitutes for the messenger's call—without it, no punishment.

A powerful objection against the Hanafi position on rational obligation is the Quranic verse: 'We do not punish until We send a messenger' (17:15). This lesson reveals how the Maturidi school answers this objection by introducing the period of reflection (muddah ta'ammul), which stands in place of the messenger. Understanding this principle is essential for grasping the Hanafi theory of taklif and its application to isolated individuals.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The objection: If aql can obligate belief, then a rational person who dies without believing would be punished even without a messenger, contradicting Quran 17:15.
The verse is: 'وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولا'.
2
Recall the Hanafi claim
The narration from Abu Hanifa states that a rational person cannot be excused for ignorance of the Creator. This seems to imply obligation without messenger.
3
Identify the answer
The answer: The obligation is not based on mere aql, but on aql plus a sufficient period of reflection (muddah ta'ammul). This period substitutes for the messenger's call.
The text says: 'مدت تأمل دعوت رسول کے منزلہ میں ہے'.
4
Trace the evidence
The evidence includes the narration itself, the rational argument of faith as perfection, and the analogy (qiyas) to Abu Hanifa's ruling on the foolish person at age 25.
The analogy: just as the foolish person after 25 years of experience is deemed mature, a person after sufficient reflection is accountable.
5
Apply the principle
Differentiate: If a person has time to reflect but does not, they are punishable. If they die before any reflection time, they are not.
The passage emphasizes variation in intellects; the period is not fixed and depends on the individual.

الزام عائد ہوگا کیونکہ عقل کو بعض افعال کے لئے مسلم حکم قرار دیا اور بعض کے لئے نہیں محکم محض ہے۔ حاصل نزاع: امام فخر الاسلام کے کلام سے مفہوم ہوتا ہے کہ ماتریدیہ، معتزلہ اور اشاعرہ کے مابین حاصل نزاع یہ ہے کہ معتزلہ کے نزدیک عقل علت موجبہ ہے حکم کے لئے اور اشعریہ کے نزدیک علت محمدرہ اور ماتریدیہ کے نزدیک عقل نہ غلت موجبہ ہے نہ علت محمد رہ بلکہ عقل اہلبیت حکم کو اور علیم وخبیر سے تعلق محکم کو واجب کرتی ہے۔
قوله وروى عن أبى حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى الخ
اس عبارت سے بعض حنفیہ کے موقف کی تائید و توثیق ہوتی ہے کہ احناف کے امام اعظم ابوحنیفہ کی روایت سے ظاہر ہے کہ عقل بعض احکام کے ادراک میں مستقل ہے چنانچہ کتاب منتقلی میں، پھر امیز ان میں محمد بین ساعت عن محمد بن الحسن اور جامع الاسرار وغیرہ میں عن ابی یوسف عن ابی حنیفہ مروی ہے کہ کوئی عاقل اپنے خالق سے جاہل رہ جانے کے سبب قیامت کے دن معذور نہیں سمجھا جائے گا بلکہ معذب ہوگا کیونکہ وہ خالق کے وجود، اس کی وحدانیت اور اس کے صفات کمالیہ کے ثبوت پر ، آسمان وز مین کی پیدائش ، اختلاف لیل و نہار و دیگر مخلوقات و عجائب قدرت اور خود اپنی خلقت جیسے دلائل و شواہد کا مشاہدہ کر رہا ہے اس لئے اسمیں قطعا شک کی گنجائش نہیں ۔
عقلاً معرفت الہی کو ثابت کرتے ہوئے اس جگہ فاضل خیر آبادی نے کہا ہے۔
حق یہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ اور اس کے صفات پر ایمان صفت کمال ہے اور اس سے کفر جمیع عقلاء کے نزدیک صفت نقصان ہے نیز ایمان شکر نعمت ہے اور شکر نعمت صفت کمال ہے اور کفر، کفران نعمت ہے اور کفران نعمت صفت نقصان ہے پس منقل کے نزدیک ایمان صفت حسن اور کفر فتیح ہو گا اس لئے لازم ہے کر عقل حسن کی طرف رغبت رکھے اور فتح سے بے رغبتی کا اظہار کرے لہذا جو بندہ امر مستحسن عقلی کو ترک کرے گا معاقب ہوگا معذور نہیں سمجھا جائے گا گر چہ اللہ عز شانہ کی طرف سے کوئی حکم نہیں آیا ہو۔
قوله اقول لعل المراد بعد مضى مدة التأمل الخ درج بالا عبارت ایک اعتراض کا جواب ہے اعتراض و جواب کی تقریر درج ذیل ہے۔ اعتراض: کی تقریر یہ ہے کہ امام اعظم ابوحنیفہ اور بعض حنفیہ کے قول پر لازم آتا ہے کہ بعثت رسول اور تبلیغ دعوت رسالت کے بغیر اللہ اور اسکی صفات پر ایمان لازم ہے لہذا کوئی شخص عاقل اگر اللہ پر ایمان لائے بغیر مر گیا تو وہ بغیر دعوت رسول معذب ہوگا کہ جہالت اپنے خالق سے اس کے حق میں عذر نہیں ہے حالانکہ اللہ تعالیٰ فرماتا ہے و ماكنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولاً جواب :۔ حاصل جواب یہ ہے کہ امام اعظم وغیرہ کے قول کا یہ معنی نہیں ہے کہ خالق پر ایمان لانے کے لئے صرف حصول عقل کافی ہے بلکہ ان کی مراد یہ ہے کہ حصول عقل کے بعد عاقل کو اتنا وقت اور زمانہ ملا کہ وہ اتنے زمانہ میں خالق کے بارے میں غور و فکر کر سکتا ہے مگر مدت تأمل گذر گیا حتی کہ وہ عاقل بے ایمان مرگیا تو معذب ہو گا گر چہ اس کے پاس دعوت رسالت نہ پہونچی کیونکہ مدت تأمل دعوت رسول کے منزلہ میں ہے جس طرح دعوت رسول اللہ کے وجود، اس کی وحدانیت اور صفات کمالیہ کے ثبوت پر قلب کو بیدار کرتی ہے اور آدمی کو آگاہ کرتی ہے اسی طرح عقل مدت تأمل میں قلب و دماغ کو متنبہ
کرتی ہے۔ ہاں انسان چونکہ مختلف الطبائع والعقول پیدا ہوا ہے سونچنے سمجھنے اور غور وفکر کے اعتبار سے ہر ایک کی عقل یکساں نہیں ہوتی بلکہ ان میں بڑا تفاوت ہوتا ہے جیسا کہ احادیث و آثار اور مشاہدات و تجربات بھی تفاوت عقول پر شاہد ہیں لہذا اگر شخص مذکور نے مدت تأمل نہ پایا کہ مر گیا تو معذب نہ ہوگا کہ مدت تأمل جو دعوت رسول کے منزلہ میں تھی نہ پاسکا۔
جواب مذکور فخر الاسلام کے اصول سے ماخوذ ہے چنانچہ انہوں نے کہا ہے
معنى قولنا يكلف بالعقل انه اذاعاته الله تعالى بالتجربة وامهله لدرک العواقب لم يكن معذوراً وان لم يبلغه الدعوة على نحو ماقال ابو حنيفة في السفيه انه اذا بلغ خمساً وعشرين سنة لا يمنع من ماله لانه قد استوفى مدة التجربة فلابد ان
يزداد رشداً وليس على الحد في هذا الباب دليل قاطع
In reality, all rulings are entailed, even though intellect perceives the beauty and ugliness of only some actions. The Maturidis have specified that intellect is independent in perceiving some rulings, but they did not say that it perceives all rulings from beauty and ugliness. However, some Hanafis will be accused of arbitrariness because they made intellect a governing [authority] for some actions and not for others. The essence of the dispute: from the words of Imam Fakhr al-Islam, it is understood that the dispute among Maturidis, Mu'tazila, and Ash'aris is that according to the Mu'tazila, intellect is an effective cause for the ruling; according to the Ash'aris, intellect is a preparatory cause; and according to the Maturidis, intellect is neither an effective cause nor a preparatory cause, rather intellect necessitates the ruling and [the ruling's] connection to the All-Knowing and All-Aware makes the ruling obligatory. His statement: 'and it is narrated from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, etc.' This phrase supports and confirms the stance of some Hanafis that from the narration of the great imam Abu Hanifa, it is apparent that intellect is independent in perceiving some rulings. Thus in the book Muntqala, then ... from Muhammad al-Shaybani, and in Jami' al-Asrar etc. from Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa, it is narrated that no rational person will be considered excused on the Day of Judgment for being ignorant of his Creator, but will be punished, because he observes the evidence and proofs of the existence of the Creator, His oneness, and His attributes of perfection—the creation of the heavens and earth, the alternation of night and day, other creatures and wonders of power, and his own creation—so there is no room for doubt in this. Establishing the knowledge of God rationally, here Fadil Khayrabadi said: The truth is that faith in Allah and His attributes is a perfection, and disbelief is a deficiency in the view of all rational people; also faith is gratitude for blessings, and gratitude is a perfection, and disbelief is ingratitude, which is a deficiency. So according to the rational person, faith is a quality of beauty and disbelief is a quality of ugliness. Therefore, it is necessary that intellect inclines toward beauty and expresses aversion to ugliness. Hence, a servant who abandons a rationally good act will be punished and not considered excused, even if no command has come from Allah. His statement: 'I say, perhaps the meaning is after the passage of the period of reflection, etc.' The above phrase is an answer to an objection. The formulation of the objection and answer is as follows. Objection: It is necessary from the statement of Imam Abu Hanifa and some Hanafis that faith in Allah and His attributes is obligatory without sending a messenger and conveying the message, so if a rational person dies without believing, he will be punished without the call of the messenger, because ignorance of his Creator is not an excuse, whereas Allah says: 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' (17:15). Answer: The summary of the answer is that the meaning of the statement of Imam Abu Hanifa etc. is not that mere attainment of reason is sufficient for faith in the Creator; rather, their intention is that after attaining reason, the person is given enough time and period in which he can reflect on the Creator, but the period of reflection passes and he dies without faith, so he will be punished even if the call of the messenger did not reach him, because the period of reflection is in the position of the call of the messenger. Just as the call of the messenger awakens the heart and informs the person about the existence of Allah, His oneness, and perfect attributes, similarly intellect in the period of reflection alerts the heart and mind. However, since humans are created with different temperaments and intellects, the intellect of each is not equal in terms of thinking, understanding, and reflection; rather, there is great variation among them, as hadiths, reports, observations, and experiences testify to the variation of intellects. Therefore, if the mentioned person did not find a period of reflection and died, he will not be punished because he did not attain the period of reflection which is in the position of the call of the messenger. The mentioned answer is derived from the principles of Fakhr al-Islam. Indeed, he said: The meaning of our statement 'he is obligated by intellect' is that when Allah gives him experience through trial and grants him time to comprehend consequences, he is not excused even if the call did not reach him. This is similar to what Abu Hanifa said about the foolish person: when he reaches twenty-five years of age, he is not prevented from his wealth, because he has completed the period of experience, so he must have increased in maturity. There is no decisive evidence on the limit in this matter.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Musallam al-Thubut (a Hanafi usul work) discusses the Maturidi position on the role of intellect (aql) in legal obligation (taklif) and the dispute with Mu'tazila and Ash'aris. The author first explains the three positions: Mu'tazila hold that aql is an effective cause (illa mujiba) for rulings; Ash'aris hold it is a preparatory cause (illa muhayyi'a); Maturidis hold it is neither but rather necessitates the ruling by making the connection to Allah necessary. Some Hanafis are criticized for inconsistency in granting aql authority only for some actions. The passage then cites a narration from Abu Hanifa (through Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) that a rational person cannot claim ignorance of the Creator because of the clear evidence in creation, implying that aql can independently know certain obligations like belief in God. Fadil Khayrabadi adds a rational argument: faith is a perfection and gratitude, disbelief is a deficiency and ingratitude; aql naturally inclines to perfection and beauty, so abandoning rationally good acts warrants punishment even without revelation. An objection is raised: this seems to contradict the Quranic verse 'We do not punish until We send a messenger' (17:15). The answer clarifies that the obligation is not based on mere aql alone, but on aql plus a sufficient period for reflection (muddah ta'ammul). This period serves as a substitute for the messenger's call. If a person has time to reflect but fails to believe, he is punishable; if he dies before having such time, he is not. This is based on Fakhr al-Islam's principle, analogized to Abu Hanifa's ruling that a foolish person reaching 25 years of age is deemed mature and cannot be prevented from his property—the period of experience suffices. Thus, the passage presents a nuanced Hanafi-Maturidi position balancing rational obligation with the condition of opportunity for reflection.

Key Terms
عقل (aql): Intellect or reason; the faculty by which humans perceive the goodness or evil of actions.
مدت تأمل (muddah ta'ammul): The period of reflection granted by Allah after attaining reason, in which a person can contemplate the existence and attributes of the Creator.
علت موجبہ (illa mujiba): Effective cause; Mu'tazila view aql as such.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi/Hanafi answer to the objection, why does the period of reflection (muddah ta'ammul) prevent contradiction with Quran 17:15?
A) Because the period of reflection is longer than the time it takes to send a messenger.
B) Because the period of reflection functions as a substitute for the messenger's call, thus providing a warning.
C) Because the verse is abrogated by the narration from Abu Hanifa.
D) Because the period of reflection is only for the foolish, not for the rational.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi/Maturidi concept of muddah ta'ammul resolves the apparent conflict between rational obligation and Quran 17:15 by treating the reflection period as a substitute for the messenger's warning.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
The Mountaintop Person: Reason vs Revelation in Obligation
Analyzing the case of a person reaching puberty on a mountaintop without da'wah across Ash'ari, Mu'tazila, and Hanafi schools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three positions (Ash'ari, Mu'tazila, majority Hanafi) regarding the mountaintop person and explain the Hanafi Maturidi reliance on the period of reflection.

Core Idea
The Hanafi Maturidi position balances reason and revelation: punishment only after a period of reflection.

The debate over husn wa qubh (intrinsic good and evil) directly shapes a classic usul scenario: the person who reaches puberty on a high mountain without any prophetic call. This case tests whether reason alone can obligate belief. Different schools answer differently, and the Hanafi (Maturidi) position offers a nuanced middle path that respects both reason and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and its key features
Understand the hypothetical: a person reaches legal maturity (bulugh) on a remote mountain peak, isolated from any prophetic message (da'wah). The question is whether he is obligated to believe in the Creator and punishable for disbelief.
This scenario is used to test theories of moral obligation.
2
State the Ash'ari position and its basis
Ash'aris hold that husn wa qubh are determined solely by revelation. Since no revelation reached the person, there is no obligation and no punishment. They reject the idea that reason alone can impose a duty.
They view actions as neutral before revelation.
3
State the Mu'tazila position and its basis
Mu'tazila hold that husn wa qubh are intrinsic to actions and knowable by reason. Therefore, the person is obligated immediately upon reaching maturity to believe in Allah and avoid disbelief, and is punishable for failure.
Some Hanafis (a minority) share this view.
4
State the majority Hanafi (Maturidi) position
The majority Hanafis hold that the person is not obligated initially because revelation is the primary source of obligation. However, after a period of reflection (muddat al-ta'ammul), reason suffices to know the Creator, and obligation sets in. Thus, punishment occurs only after that period.
This is a middle ground: reason can obligate but only after a chance to reflect.
5
Compare the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Ash'ari: no punishment ever. Mu'tazila/some Hanafis: punishment immediately. Majority Hanafis: punishment only after the reflection period passes.
The difference hinges on whether and when reason can establish obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله-بما حررنا من المذاهب يتفرع عليه الخ
اس عبارت سے مصنف کہنا چاہتے ہیں کہ حسن و قبیح کے ذیل میں مذاہب اشعریہ معتزلہ اور ماتریدیہ حنفیہ کے بیان کر دینے سے البالغ فی شاهق الجبل کا مسئلہ متفرع ہو جاتا ہے۔ چنانچہ وہ بچہ جو پہاڑ کی چوٹی پر جوان ہوا اور اس تک کسی طرح دعوت رسالت نہ پہونچ سکی تو وہ بر بنائے مذہب اشعریہ معذور ہوگا ، بعض حنفیہ اور معتزلہ کے نزدیک مکلف ہے اس لئے ترک ایمان کے سبب معاقب ہوگا اور جمہور حنفیہ کے نزدیک تکلیف کی اہلیت نہ ہونے کے سبب مکلف نہ ہوگا تا وقت کہ مدت تأمل گذر جائے لہذا قبل تأمل معذب نہ ہوگا مگر بعد مدت تأمل معذب ہوگا۔
اس جگہ مصنف نے حاشیہ میں کہا ہے۔
من بلغ في الجبال الشاهقة ولم يبلغله الدعوة فلم يعتقد بالعقائد ولم
يعمل بالشرائع فعند المعتزلة وطائفة من الحنفية يعاقب في الآخرة لتركه مايستقل به العقل وعند الاشاعرة وجمهور الحنفية لا يعاقب لان الحكم انما هو بالشرع وقد فرض انه لم يبلغه انتهت ۔ یعنی جو شخص شاہقہ جبال میں بالغ ہوا اور اس تک دعوت رسالت نہ پہونچ سکی پس اس نے عقائد ( دینیہ ) پر اعتقاد رکھا اور نہ شرائع اسلامیہ پر عمل کیا تو معتزلہ اور حنفیہ کے ایک گروہ کے نزدیک آخرت میں معاقب ہوگا کیونکہ اس نے ان احکام کو ترک کیا جن کے ادراک کرنے میں عقل مستقل ہے البتہ اشاعرہ اور جمہور حنفیہ کے نزدیک معاقب نہیں ہوگا اس لئے کہ حکم شرع ہی سے معلوم ہو سکتا ہے حالانکہ فرض کیا گیا ہے کہ اس تک حکم شرع نہیں پہونچا۔ معتزلہ کے نزدیک شخص مذکور آخرت میں معاقب اس لئے ہوگا کہ ان کے نزدیک حسن و قبح بالمعنی المذ کو فعل کے لئے ذاتی ہیں اور درود شرع کی استعانت کے بغیر مستلزم حکم بھی ہیں لہذا شاهق جبل کے بالغ ایسے ہی صبی عاقل پر ایمان لانا واجب ہوگا اور کفر حرام رہے گا مگر ایمان نہیں لایا اس لئے ترک ایمان کے سبب اور کفر کو ترجیح دینے کے سبب دونوں معذب ہوں گے بلکہ ایمان و کفر میں سے کچھ بھی عقیدہ نہ بنایا پھر بھی معذب ہوں گے کیونکہ انہوں نے اس حکم کو ترک کیا جو ان پر عقلاً واجب تھا۔ اشعریہ کے نزدیک حسن و قبح چونکہ ذوات افعال سے نہیں ہیں بلکہ شرعی ہیں پس ورود شرع سے پہلے ان کے نزدیک افعال میں کوئی حکم ہی نہیں ہے
His statement: 'From what we have recorded of the schools, it branches off, etc.' The author intends by this phrase that from the exposition of the schools of the Ash'ariyya, Mu'tazila, and Maturidiyya Hanafiyya under the topic of husn wa qubh, the issue of al-baligh fi shahiq al-jabal (the one who reaches puberty on a high mountain) branches off. Thus, the child who grew up on a mountain peak and the call of the messenger did not reach him in any way: according to the Ash'ari school, he is excused; according to some Hanafis and the Mu'tazila, he is obligated and will be punished for leaving faith; and according to the majority of Hanafis, he is not obligated due to lacking the capacity for taklif until the period of reflection passes, so he will not be punished before reflection, but after the period of reflection he will be punished.
At this point the author said in the marginalia: 'Whoever reaches puberty in the high mountains and the call does not reach him, so he does not believe in the doctrines nor act upon the laws—according to the Mu'tazila and a group of Hanafis, he will be punished in the Hereafter for abandoning what reason independently comprehends; according to the Ash'ariyya and the majority of Hanafis, he will not be punished because the ruling is only by revelation, and it has been assumed that it did not reach him.' That is, the person who reached puberty in high mountains and the call of the messenger did not reach him, so he neither believed in religious doctrines nor acted upon Islamic laws: according to the Mu'tazila and one group of Hanafis, he will be punished in the Hereafter because he abandoned those rulings which reason can independently comprehend; however, according to the Ash'ariyya and the majority of Hanafis, he will not be punished because the ruling can only be known through revelation, and it has been assumed that revelation did not reach him. For the Mu'tazila, the mentioned person will be punished in the Hereafter because, according to them, husn wa qubh in the aforementioned sense are intrinsic to actions and necessitate a ruling without the aid of revelation. Hence, for the one who reaches puberty on a high mountain, just as for a rational child, belief in Allah is obligatory and disbelief is forbidden; since he did not believe, he will be punished for leaving faith and for preferring disbelief. Even if he formed no belief of faith or disbelief, he will still be punished because he abandoned the ruling that was rationally obligatory upon him. For the Ash'ariyya, since husn wa qubh are not from the essences of actions but rather from revelation, therefore, before the arrival of revelation, according to them, there is no ruling in actions at all.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage addresses a famous usul al-fiqh dispute about the source of moral obligation, branching from the discussion of husn wa qubh (intrinsic good and evil). The author presents the case of a person who grows up on a remote mountain summit and never receives any prophetic message. Three positions are given: (1) The Ash'ari position: this person is excused entirely because obligation (taklif) depends on revelation, which never reached him. (2) The Mu'tazili position (and some Hanafis): this person is obligated and punishable because reason alone can discern good and evil, and thus obligations like faith in the Creator are rationally binding. (3) The majority Hanafi (Maturidi) position: this person is not obligated until the period of reflection passes; before that, he is excused, but after sufficient time for reflection, he becomes accountable and would be punished. The text clarifies that the majority Hanafis agree with Ash'aris that before the period of reflection, he is not punished, but disagree after that period. The marginalia further explains that the Mu'tazila hold that husn wa qubh are intrinsic and rationally known, so failure to believe is punishable; Ash'aris hold that only revelation gives ruling, so no punishment; majority Hanafis hold a middle ground: reason can obligate after reflection, but revelation is the primary source of legal rulings.

Key Terms
حسن و قبح (husn wa qubh): The classification of actions as inherently good or evil, knowable by reason without revelation.
شاهق الجبل (shahiq al-jabal): The specific case of a person who reaches legal maturity on a high mountain peak isolated from any prophetic message.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi (Maturidi) position in this passage, what is the ruling for a person who reaches puberty on a remote mountain without receiving any prophetic call?
A) He will be punished immediately for not believing
B) He will never be punished because revelation never reached him
C) He will not be punished before the period of reflection, but will be punished after it passes
D) He will be rewarded for his patience

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi Maturidi position on the mountaintop person demonstrates a balanced usul principle: reason can obligate, but only after a period of reflection when revelation is absent.
