Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
10 min
The Hanafi Position on Moral Rationality and the Mountain Dweller
Explaining the dispute on husn/qubh and the mountain dweller's accountability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three major positions on the accountability of a person unreached by revelation (al-baligh fi shahiq al-jabal) and explain the Hanafi proof from universal agreement on husn and qubh.

Core Idea
Universal rational agreement on goodness and evil shows moral knowledge does not depend on revelation alone.

This passage from Sharh Muslim al-Thubut presents a classic usul al-fiqh dispute over the basis of moral valuation (husn and qubh). The author first shows how this abstract theoretical issue yields a concrete legal case: what is the ruling on a person who reaches puberty in complete isolation without ever receiving the prophetic message? The answer depends on one's school regarding the role of reason. The passage then recites a key Hanafi proof from universal agreement and explains why it does not entail that rational judgment alone imposes a binding divine ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concrete legal case
The dispute revolves around the mountain dweller (al-baligh fi shahiq al-jabal) who never receives the prophetic message. Determine what each school says about his accountability in the afterlife.
This is the far' that tests the theoretical principles.
2
Map the three schools' positions on the mountain dweller
Mu'tazila and some Hanafis: immediately punishable. Ash'ariyya and majority Hanafis: excused permanently. Maturidi/Hanafi majority (including Imam Abu Hanifa): given a period of reflection (tammul), then punishable if he does not believe.
Note that the Ash'ari position is the opposite of Mu'tazila; Hanafis take a middle path.
3
Identify the underlying principle causing the difference
The disagreement stems from the nature of husn (goodness) and qubh (evil): are they intrinsic to acts or determined by revelation? Mu'tazila say intrinsic and directly obligate; Ash'ariyya say only from revelation; Hanafis say some are rationally known but full obligation comes through revelation.
This is the theoretical root of the ikhtilaf.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof from universal agreement
The matn of Muslim al-Thubut argues: 'The goodness of beneficence and the evil of its opposite are things upon which all rational people agree, even those who reject messengers like the Brahmins. If it were not intrinsic, they would not agree.' This shows rational knowledge of some moral values independent of revelation.
The proof is an ijma' of rational persons (ijma' al-'uqala').
5
Understand the Hanafi response to the objection
The opponent might say this agreement could be due to a general maslaha. The Hanafi scholar responds: that does not harm us, because we do not claim that this rational agreement necessitates a divine ruling; we only claim it shows the value is not dependent on revelation. The actual reward/punishment comes from sam' (revelation).
This preserves the balance between reason and revelation.

اشرف النعوت شرح مسلم الثبوت
دعوت رسالت ان کے پاس امرایمان کا حکم وجوبی نہیں پہونچا ہے لہذا دونوں معذور ہوں گے ترک ایمان کے سبب معاقب نہ ہونگے اسی طرف علماء بخار ابھی گئے ہیں۔ امام ابو منصور ماتریدی وغیرہ علماء کبار کے نزدیک شخص مذکور کا حکم بین بین ہے جن افعال کا حکم عقل مستقلا ادراک کر سکتی ہے جیسے ایمان، شکر منعم اور کفر و کفران نعمت و غیرہ تو وہ دونوں قبل بعثت ان احکام کے مکلف ہیں اسلئے کہ وجوب حکم کی علت عقل موجود ہے لہذا ترک کے باعث معذب ہونگے ۔
اور امام اعظم ابوحنیفہ اور فخر الاسلام وغیرھا کے نزدیک مدت تأمل گذرنے کے بعد بھی ایمان نہیں لایا کہ مر گیا تو معذب ہوگا کہ مدت تأمل تنبیہ قلب میں دعوت رسالت کے منزل میں ہے۔ واللہ تعالیٰ اعلم علمہ اتم واحکم ۔
متن المسلم : لنا أن حسن الاحسان وقبح مقابلته بالاساءة مما اتفق عليه العقلاء حتى من لا يقول بارسال الرسول كالبراهمة فلولا أنه ذاتى لم يكن كذالك والجواب بانه يجوز ان يكون لمصلحة عامة لايضرنا لان رعاية المصلحة العامة حسن بالضرورة وانما يضرنا لوادعينا انه لذات الفعل بل لدعوى عدم التوقف على الشرع ومنع الاتفاق على انه مناط حكمه تعالى لايمسنا فانا لا نقول باستلزامه حكمامنه تعالى بل ذالك بالسمع
ترجمہ :۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ احسان (حسن وسلوک کرنا ) کا حسن ہونا اور اس کے برخلاف اساء ة ( برائی کرنا ) کا قبیح ہونا ان چیزوں میں سے ہے جس پر سارے عقلاء کا اتفاق ہے حتی کہ وہ لوگ بھی جو رسول کی بعثت کے قائل نہیں ہیں جیسے براہمہ۔ لہذا اگر وہ ذاتی نہ ہوتا تو اتفاق نہ ہوتا اور یہ جواب کہ ممکن ہے کہ کسی مصلحت عامہ کی وجہ سے ہو ہمارے مضر نہیں کیونکہ مصلحت عامہ کی رعایت بالبداہت حسن ہے ہاں یہ جواب ہمکو اس وقت ضرور دیتا جب ہم دعوی کرتے کہ حسن و قتح ذات فعل کی وجہ سے ہے بلکہ هما را دعوئی تو یہ ہے کہ حسن و فتح شرع پر موقوف نہیں ہے اور اس کے مفاد حکم الہی ہونے پر اتفاق ہونے کو منع کرنا ہم کو مس نہیں کرتا اس لئے کہ ہم حسن و فتح میں سے کسی کو اللہ تعالیٰ کے کسی حکم کے لئے مستلزم نہیں مانتے بلکہ وہ ہمارے نزدیک سمعی ہے۔
The author says: The discussion of the mountain dweller (al-baligh fi shahiq al-jabal) branches off from our exposition of the schools of Ash'ariyya, Mu'tazila, and Maturidiyya/Hanafiyya regarding husn and qubh. Thus, a child who reaches puberty on a mountain peak without the prophetic message reaching him—according to the Ash'ari school he is excused; according to some Hanafis and the Mu'tazila he is obligated (mukallaf) and will be punished for abandoning faith; according to the majority of Hanafis he is not obligated until a period of reflection (tammul) passes; he will not be punished before reflection but will be punished after it. In the margin, the author says: 'Whoever reaches puberty in high mountains without the call reaching him, and does not believe in the creeds nor act on the laws—according to the Mu'tazila and a group of Hanafis, he will be punished in the afterlife for abandoning what reason independently perceives; according to the Ash'ariyya and the majority of Hanafis, he will not be punished because judgment comes only from the Law (shar'), and it is assumed that the Law did not reach him.' That is, the Mu'tazila and a group of Hanafis hold that the mentioned person is punished because husn and qubh are intrinsic to acts and entail reward/punishment without need for revelation; thus the mountain dweller is obligated to have faith and must avoid disbelief. The Ash'ariyya hold that husn and qubh are not intrinsic but from the Law, so before revelation, acts have no ruling, and he is excused. Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and other senior scholars hold an intermediate position: for acts that reason can independently judge (like faith, gratitude to the benefactor), he is obligated before revelation; for others, not. Imam Abu Hanifa and Fakhr al-Islam hold that after a period of reflection, if he dies without faith, he is punished because that period substitutes for the call. The text of al-Muslim: 'Our proof: that the goodness of ihsan and the evil of responding with isa'a are things upon which all rational people agree, even those who do not affirm the sending of messengers, like the Brahmins. If it were not intrinsic, this would not be the case. The answer that it may be due to a general maslaha does not harm us because observing the general maslaha is necessarily good. It would harm us only if we claimed it is due to the act's essence, but we only claim that it is not dependent on revelation. Denying that it is a basis for Allah's ruling does not affect us because we do not say it necessitates a ruling from Him; rather that is from hearing (sam').' Translation: Our proof is that the goodness of doing good and the evil of doing evil are among matters agreed upon by all rational people, even those who do not believe in the sending of messengers like the Brahmins. If it were not intrinsic, there would be no agreement. The answer that it might be due to a general maslaha does not harm us because observing the general maslaha is evidently good. This answer would harm us if we claimed that it is due to the essence of the act, but our claim is that it is not dependent on revelation. Denying that it is a basis for Allah's ruling does not affect us because we do not believe that it entails a ruling from Allah; rather that is from revelation.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Muslim al-Thubut discusses the classical usul al-fiqh dispute over whether moral values (husn and qubh) are intrinsic to acts or determined by revelation. The author first shows how this dispute yields a concrete furu‘ issue: the person who reaches puberty in isolation without receiving the prophetic message. The passage then outlines the positions of the major schools: the Mu‘tazila and some Hanafis hold that reason alone obligates faith, so such a person is punished; the Ash‘ariyya and the majority of Hanafis deny obligation before revelation, so he is excused; and the Maturidi and other Hanafi authorities (including Imam Abu Hanifa himself) take an intermediate view—after a period of reflection, he becomes obligated. The author then quotes the matn (the original text of Muslim al-Thubut) which presents a rational proof for the intrinsic nature of husn and qubh: universal agreement among rational people, even non-Muslims like the Brahmins, that goodness and evil are real. The matn anticipates the objection that this agreement could stem from a general maslaha, and responds that this does not undermine the point—it only confirms that these values are immediately known, not that they are necessarily a direct basis for divine command. The commentator explains that the Hanafi position does not claim that rational judgment necessitates a ruling from Allah; rather, it holds that the shar‘ is needed for the actual ruling to become binding. This nuanced view distinguishes the Hanafis from both the Mu‘tazila (who overestimate reason) and the Ash‘ariyya (who underestimate it).

Key Terms
حسن (ḥusn): The quality of being good or praiseworthy in an act, which rational persons recognize inherently, according to the proof of universal agreement.
قبح (qubḥ): The quality of being evil or blameworthy in an act, recognized universally by rational persons as intrinsic (e.g., ingratitude).
ذات الفعل (dhāt al-fi‘l): The essence of the act, to which husn or qubh is attributed intrinsically by those who deny dependence on revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafis (Imam Abu Hanifa and Fakhr al-Islam), what is the ruling on the person who reaches puberty on a mountain unreached by the prophetic message?
A) He is punished immediately for disbelief.
B) He is given a period of reflection, and if he does not believe after that, he is punished.
C) He is never punished because revelation never reached him.
D) He is rewarded for his isolation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that reason can recognize some moral values independently, but the full binding ruling comes through revelation, resulting in a period of reflection for those unreached.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Obligation of Faith Before Prophethood: A Hanafi Internal Dispute
Internal Hanafi ikhtilaf on rational obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Hanafi positions on taklif before prophethood and explain how each uses 'aql and the absence of a prophetic call.

Core Idea
Reason alone may obligate faith, but Abu Hanifa requires a period of reflection as a substitute for prophethood.

The question of whether people before the arrival of a prophet are obligated to believe in Allah is a deep usul al-fiqh problem. Within the Hanafi school, there is a substantive internal disagreement, drawing on the role of reason ('aql) and the necessity of a prophetic warning. This lesson examines three distinct positions presented in the passage, each with its own understanding of when obligation begins.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Determine the precise question: Are people who lived before any prophetic call received obligated to have faith (iman) and be punished for disbelief?
The issue is taklif before bu'that (prophetic mission).
2
State the first position (Bukhara scholars)
Describe the view that no obligation exists because the command of faith never reached them. They are excused.
This position emphasizes the absence of a divine command.
3
Analyze the second position (Maturidi)
Explain that Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi holds that for acts reason can independently know (like faith and gratitude), the presence of 'aql itself creates obligation, even without a prophet.
Reason ('aql) is the 'illa (cause) of obligation for these acts.
4
Examine the third position (Abu Hanifa)
Imam Abu Hanifa adds a condition: after a period of reflection (ta'ammul), if one still does not believe, punishment follows. This period substitutes for the prophetic call.
The ta'ammul compensates for the absence of a direct prophetic warning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشرف النعوت شرح مسلم الثبوت دعوت رسالت ان کے پاس امرایمان کا حکم وجوبی نہیں پہونچا ہے لہذا دونوں معذور ہوں گے ترک ایمان کے سبب معاقب نہ ہونگے اسی طرف علماء بخار ابھی گئے ہیں۔ امام ابو منصور ماتریدی وغیرہ علماء کبار کے نزدیک شخص مذکور کا حکم بین بین ہے جن افعال کا حکم عقل مستقلا ادراک کر سکتی ہے جیسے ایمان، شکر منعم اور کفر و کفران نعمت و غیرہ تو وہ دونوں قبل بعثت ان احکام کے مکلف ہیں اسلئے کہ وجوب حکم کی علت عقل موجود ہے لہذا ترک کے باعث معذب ہونگے ۔ اور امام اعظم ابوحنیفہ اور فخر الاسلام وغیرھا کے نزدیک مدت تأمل گذرنے کے بعد بھی ایمان نہیں لایا کہ مر گیا تو معذب ہوگا کہ مدت تأمل تنبیہ قلب میں دعوت رسالت کے منزل میں ہے۔ واللہ تعالیٰ اعلم علمہ اتم واحکم ۔
The call of prophethood did not reach them regarding the command of faith with an obligatory ruling, so both will be excused and will not be punished for abandoning faith. The scholars of Bukhara have inclined to this side. According to Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and other great scholars, the ruling for the mentioned person is between the two. For actions whose ruling reason can independently perceive, such as faith, gratitude to the Benefactor, disbelief and ingratitude for blessings, etc., they are obligated both before prophethood because the cause for obligation—reason—is present, so they will be punished for abandoning it. According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Fakhr al-Islam and others, if after a period of reflection one does not bring faith and dies, he will be punished, because the period of reflection, in terms of alerting the heart, is in the place of the call of prophethood. And Allah knows best, His knowledge is most complete and most wise.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents three positions within the Hanafi school regarding the obligation of faith (iman) before the arrival of a prophetic call. The first, attributed to the scholars of Bukhara, holds that since no command of faith reached them, they are excused. The second, from Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, distinguishes between actions that reason can independently perceive (like faith in the Creator and gratitude) and those that require revelation; for the former, people are obligated even before prophethood. The third, from Imam Abu Hanifa, adds a condition: if a person has a period of reflection (ta'ammul) and still does not believe, he will be punished because that period serves as a substitute for the prophetic call. This internal ikhtilaf highlights the complexity of rational obligation (taklif 'aqli) in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
عقل ('aql): Reason or intellect, considered by Maturidi as the cause ('illa) for obligation of rationally knowable acts.
بعثت (bi'that): The sending of a prophet or messenger.
مکلف (mukallaf): A person who is legally obligated to perform or refrain from an act.
تأمل (ta'ammul): Period of reflection, considered by Abu Hanifa as a substitute for the prophetic call.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa (ra) regarding a person who, after a period of reflection, does not bring faith and dies?
A) He will be excused and not punished.
B) He will be punished because the period of reflection serves as a substitute for the prophetic call.
C) He will not be punished because no command reached him.
D) He will be punished only if he disbelieved after knowing the truth.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school contains an internal dispute on taklif before prophethood, with positions ranging from no obligation to obligation based on reason alone or after a period of reflection.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Hanafi Response to Ash'ari on Rational Moral Goodness
Hanafi rebuttal of Ash'ari claim that moral judgments derive from general welfare

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the core Hanafi claim about rational moral goodness and explain how the Hanafi response refutes the Ash'ari objection without requiring goodness to be intrinsic.

Core Idea
Moral goodness and badness are knowable by reason, not dependent on revelation—even if they are based on general welfare.

This passage presents a critical exchange in usul al-fiqh between the Hanafi and Ash'ari schools on the epistemological basis of moral judgments. The Ash'aris argue that the universal agreement on the goodness of benevolence is due to general welfare, not intrinsic qualities. The Hanafi author demonstrates that this very appeal to welfare confirms the rationality of moral judgment, since even non-believers agree on it. Understanding this debate clarifies the Hanafi position on the role of reason in law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'ari objection
The Ash'aris accept consensus on husn/qubh but deny it is due to intrinsic rationality; they attribute it to maslaha 'amma (general welfare).
Their objection aims to show that moral judgments are not rationally necessary, only contingent on benefit.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response on maslaha
If maslaha is judged good by all rational people (including non-Muslims), then that judgment is itself rational, not revealed. The Hanafi author says: 'رعایت مصلحت عامہ... بالبداہۃ حسن ہے'.
The Ash'ari premise actually supports the Hanafi claim.
3
Clarify the Hanafi claim
Hanafis do not claim husn/qubh are inherent in acts; only that they are not dependent on revelation. Therefore the Ash'ari objection does not harm.
The text explicitly says: 'ہم ذات فعل سے ہونے کے مدعی نہیں'.
4
Examine the second Ash'ari denial
The denial that there is consensus on necessity of divine ruling from husn/qubh is rebutted by the author's statement 'لا یمنا'.
The Hanafi author maintains that even if the direct link is denied, the rationality of moral judgments is still established.
5
Conclude the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical implication: reason can establish obligation/recommendation without explicit revealed text, while Ash'aris require a revealed command.
This affects ijtihad in cases of textual silence.

Source Text

قوله والجواب بانه يجوزان يكون لمصلحة عامة. اس عبارت سے حنفیہ کی دلیل مذکور کا جواب اشاعرہ کی طرف سے ہے، اشاعرہ کہتے ہیں کہ احسان کے حسن اور اساءت کے قبح پر عقلاء کے اتفاق کو ہم تسلیم کرتے ہیں لیکن یہ نہیں تسلیم کرتے ہیں کہ عقلاء کا یہ اتفاق حسن و قبح کے ذاتی و عقلی ہونے کی وجہ سے ہے بلکہ احسان میں چونکہ جمیع خلائق کے لئے مصلحت عامہ وابستہ ہے اس لئے عقل نے رعایت مفاد عامہ کے پیش نظر احسان میں حسن ہونے کا حکم لگا دیا اور اساءت میں چونکہ مصلحت عامہ کا نقص و مفسدہ ہے اس لئے عقل نے اس کے قبیح ہونے کا حکم لگایا بہر حال احسان و اساءت کا حسن و قبح ذات فعل کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے بلکہ مصلحت عامہ کی رعایت و عدم رعایت کی وجہ سے ہے۔
قوله لايضر نالان رعاية المصلحة العامة الخ. اس عبارت سے مصنف اشاعرہ کے جواب کا جواب دینا چاہتے ہیں اس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ اشاعرہ کا جواب مذکور ہم معاشر حنفیہ کے لئے مضر نہیں ہے اسلئے کہ اب ہم رعایت مصلحت عامہ پر کلام کریں گے کہ رعایت مصلحت عامہ متدین، غیر متدین بھی ارباب عقل و فہم کے نزدیک بالبداہۃ حسن ہے پس رعایت مصلحت عامہ اگر عقلی نہ ہو کر شرعی ہوتی تو غیر متدین و منکرین شرع ہرگز رعایت مصلحت عامہ کے حسن ہونے پر اتفاق نہ کرتے لیکن چونکہ ان سبھوں کا اتفاق ہے تو ماننا پڑے گا کہ رعایت مصلحت عامہ عقلی ہے اور جب بعض افعال کا عقلی ہونا ثابت ہو گیا تو جمیع افعال میں حسن و قبح کا عقلی ہونا ثابت ہوا اس لئے عقلی یا شرعی ہونے پر ہی اجماع ہے کوئی قول بالفصل کا قائل نہیں۔
ہاں اشاعرہ کا جواب ہمارے لئے اس وقت مضر ہوتا جب ہم دعوی کرتے کہ حسن و قبح ذات فعل کی وجہ سے ہے چنانچہ اس وقت کہا جا سکتا تھا کہ فعل احسان کا حسن اور فعل اساءت کا قبح ذات فعل سے ہونا ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے بلکہ حسن مصلحت عامہ کی رعایت کی وجہ سے اور قبح مصلحت عامہ کے نقص کی وجہ سے ہے حالانکہ ہم ذات فعل سے ہونے کے مدعی نہیں کہ ہم کو ضرر ہو۔ بلکہ ہمارا دعوی یہ ہے کہ افعال کا حسن و قبح ورود شرع پر موقوف نہیں ہے اب خواہ اس کا ثبوت ذات فعل سے ہو، صفت لازمہ سے ہو یا صفت اعتباریہ سے ہو خواہ رعایت مصلحت عامہ کی وجہ سے ہو غرض یہ کہ ثبوت حسن و قبح شرع سے نہیں ہے اگر چہ اس کے علاوہ کسی دوسری شے سے ہے تو مضائقہ نہیں، پس اس توضیح پر کوئی شبہ نہیں رہ جاتا کہ رعایت مصلحت عامہ کا حسن اور نقص مصلحت کا قبح عام ذاتی عقلی ہے شرعی نہیں۔
قوله ومنع الاتفاق على انه مناط حكمه الخ. اس عبارت سے مصنف نے حسن و قبح کے عقلی ہونے میں حنفیہ کی دلیل پر اشاعرہ کا منع ذکر کیا ہے اور پھر اشاعرہ کے منع کا جواب دیا ہے۔ منع: دلیل مذکور پر اشاعرہ منع وارد کرتے ہوئے کہتے ہیں کہ حسن و قبح کے موجب حکم الہی ہونے پر عقلاء کے متفق ہونے کو ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے یعنی ہم نہیں مانتے کہ حسن و قبح سے مستلزم حکم ہونے پر سارے عقلاء کا اتفاق ہے۔ مصنف نے اپنے قول لا یمنا سے اشاعرہ کے منع کا جواب دیا ہے۔
His statement: 'And the answer is that it is permissible that it be for a general benefit.' This phrase is the answer of the Ash'aris to the mentioned proof of the Hanafis. The Ash'aris say: We accept the consensus of rational people on the goodness of benevolence and the badness of harm, but we do not accept that this consensus is due to goodness and badness being intrinsic and rational. Rather, because in benevolence a general benefit is linked to all creatures, reason judged it good out of regard for common interest, and in harm, because there is a loss and corruption of general benefit, reason judged it bad. In any case, the goodness and badness of benevolence and harm are not due to the act itself but due to regard for general benefit or its lack. His statement: 'It does not harm us because regard for general welfare...' Through this phrase, the author intends to answer the Ash'ari response. The summary is that the mentioned Ash'ari response is not harmful to us Hanafis, because we will now discuss regard for general welfare: the observance of general welfare is, by necessity, good in the view of rational and understanding people, both religious and non-religious. So if regard for general welfare were not rational but revealed, then non-religious people and deniers of revelation would never agree on its goodness. But since all of them agree, it must be accepted that regard for general welfare is rational. And when the rationality of some acts is established, the rationality of goodness and badness in all acts is established. Therefore, there is consensus on its being rational or revealed; no one holds a differentiating opinion. Yes, the Ash'ari response would harm us if we claimed that goodness and badness are due to the act itself. At that time it could be said: 'We do not accept that the goodness of benevolence and badness of harm are due to the act itself; rather, goodness is due to regard for general welfare and badness due to its loss.' But we do not claim that they are due to the act itself, so that we would be harmed. Rather, our claim is that the goodness and badness of acts are not dependent on the advent of revelation. Now, whether their proof is from the act itself, from an inherent attribute, or from an attributive attribute, or due to regard for general welfare — in short, the proof of goodness and badness is not from revelation, even if from some other thing, there is no harm. After this clarification, no doubt remains that the goodness of regard for general welfare and the badness of loss of welfare are generally intrinsic and rational, not revealed. His statement: 'And denial of consensus that it is the basis of His ruling...' Through this phrase, the author mentions the Ash'ari denial of the Hanafi proof for the rationality of goodness and badness, and then responds to it. The denial: The Ash'aris raise a denial against the mentioned proof, saying: We do not accept that rational people are agreed that goodness and badness necessitate divine ruling — that is, we do not accept that there is consensus of all rational people that goodness and badness entail ruling. The author responds to the Ash'ari denial with his statement 'la yumna' (it is not prevented).
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents a key dispute in usul al-fiqh between the Hanafis and the Ash'aris regarding the rational basis of moral judgments (husn and qubh). The Ash'aris argue that the universal agreement on the goodness of benevolence and badness of harm is not due to these qualities being intrinsic to acts, but because of the general welfare (maslaha 'amma) associated with them. They claim that reason judges based on benefit and harm, not on the acts themselves. The Hanafi author responds by pointing out that the Ash'ari position actually supports the Hanafi stance: if even non-religious people agree that regard for general welfare is good, this consensus itself proves that such moral judgments are rational, not merely revealed. The Hanafis do not insist that goodness/badness are inherent in the acts (bi-dhat al-fi'l); they only assert that these judgments are not dependent on revelation. The Ash'aris also deny that there is consensus that goodness/badness necessitate divine command, but the author counters this denial. Throughout, the author follows the order of the Ash'ari objection, then the Hanafi response, and finally the denial and rejoinder. The discussion is dense and requires careful tracking of who is arguing what.

Key Terms
حسن و قبح (husn wa qubh): Moral goodness and badness of actions, as determined by reason or revelation.
مصلحت عامة (maslaha 'amma): General welfare or common benefit that is the basis for rational judgment of goodness.
ذاتی عقلی (dhati 'aqli): Intrinsic and rational, meaning the moral quality belongs to the act itself and is known by reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, what is the main point of disagreement with the Ash'aris?
A) Whether moral judgments are intrinsic to acts or based on general welfare
B) Whether moral judgments are known by reason or only by revelation
C) Whether general welfare is a valid source of law
D) Whether there is consensus on the goodness of benevolence

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that moral judgments are rational and not dependent on revelation, even if their basis is general welfare.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
The Hanafi Proof for Rational Good and Evil (Husn and Qubh)
Responding to Ash'ari objections with rational proofs for husn and qubh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi rational proofs for the rationality of ḥusn and qubḥ and evaluate the Ashʿari objections using the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Good and evil are known rationally, not solely by revelation—a foundational branch of Hanafi uṣūl.

This passage tackles one of the most fundamental debates in uṣūl al-fiqh: are moral values (ḥusn and qubḥ) known by reason or only by revelation? The author, a Hanafi commentator, defends the rationality of moral values against Ashʿari objections. We will walk through the three principal Hanafi proofs and the Ashʿari counterarguments, and see how the Hanafi position is sustained without claiming that values necessitate divine rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Point of Dispute
Determine what exactly is being contested: the source of knowledge of ḥusn and qubḥ, not whether they exist.
Both schools agree that ḥusn and qubḥ exist; the disagreement is epistemic.
2
Analyze the First Proof: Public Interest
The text argues that even non-religious rational people agree that observing public interest (maṣlaḥa) is good, proving its rationality.
This proof relies on universal consensus of people of intellect.
3
Analyze the Second Proof: Preponderance without Preponderant
If ḥusn and qubḥ were only revealed, before revelation all acts would be equal; God's preference of one over another would be arbitrary, contradicting divine wisdom.
This proof is based on the impossibility of tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ.
4
Analyze the Third Proof: Prophetic Mission
If morality were purely revelational, sending prophets would be a trial (balā') rather than a mercy (raḥma), because they bring accountability and punishment.
This proof argues from the purpose of prophethood.
5
Evaluate the Ashʿari Denial and Hanafi Response
The Ashʿaris deny that consensus on public interest proves rationality and reject that ḥusn and qubḥ necessitate divine rulings. The Hanafi response clarifies that they do not claim necessitation; the proof is only for rationality of value, not for derivation of rulings.
The final matn from Muslim al-Thubut adds a nuance about the impossibility of true equality in the external world.

قوله ومنع الاتفاق على انه مناط حكمه الخ اس عبارت سے مصنف نے حسن و قبح کے عقلی ہونے میں حنفیہ کی دلیل پر اشاعرہ کا منع ذکر کیا ہے اور پھر اشاعرہ کے منع کا جواب دیا ہے۔ منع ۔ دلیل مذکور پر اشاعرہ منع وارد کرتے ہوئے کہتے ہیں کہ حسن وقیح کے موجب حکم الہی ہونے پر عقلاء کے متفق ہونے کو ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے یعنی ہم نہیں مانتے کہ حسن و فتح سے مستلزم حکم ہونے پر سارے عقلاء کا اتفاق ہے۔ مصنف نے اپنے قول لا یمنا سے اشاعرہ کے منع کا جواب دیا ہے حاصل یہ ہے کہ یہ منع ہمارے دعوئی کو مس تک نہیں کر رہا ہے اس لئے کہ ہم خود حسن و فتح کو ستلزم حکم نہیں مانتے چہ جائیکہ اتفاق کا قول کریں بلکہ ہم بھی حکم کو شروع ہی سے ثابت مانتے ہیں رہا دلیل کا ذکر تو اس میں صرف حسن و قبیح کے عقلی ہونے کا اثبات سے پس اس حیثیت سے یہ منع ان لوگوں پر بھی وارد نہیں ہوگا جو حسن و فتح کوستلزم حکم مانتے ہیں کیونکہ جس دلیل پر منع وارد کیا گیا ہے وہ دلیل استلزام حکم پر قائم نہیں کی گئی ہے بلکہ عقلیت حسن و فتح پر قائم ہے۔
دلیل (۲): حسن وقیح کے عقلی ہونے پر بعض اعاظم نے دلیل دیتے ہوئے کہا ہے کہ حسن و قبح اگر شرعی ہوں تو نماز وز نا میں کوئی فرق نہیں رہ جائگا بلکہ نفس الامر میں دونوں ایک دوسرے کے مساوی ہو جائیں گے ( بعثت رسول سے پیشتر ) پس ان میں سے ایک کو واجب قرار دینا اور دوسرے کو حرام ٹھہرانا کوئی ان میں سے اپنی ضد سے اولی نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ دونوں مساوی ہیں پھر ترجیح بلا مرجح لازم آئے گا کہ مساوی ہونے کی صورت میں ایک کو واجب اور دوسرے کو حرام قرار کیوں دیا نیز حکمت آمر کے منافی ہے حالانکہ وہ حکیم مطلق قطعی ہے۔
دلیل (۳): حسن و فتح اگر شرعی ہوں تو رسول کی بعثتبلاء وفتنہ اور مصیبت ہوگی رحمت نہیں ہوگی کیونکہ بعثت رسول سے پیشتر لوگ آرام وسکون اور چین میں تھے کہ ان چیزوں میں مواخذہ نہیں تھا جنکے ذریعہ انسان لذت حاصل کرتا تھا لیکن رسول کی بعثت کے بعد بعض افعال کے ارتکاب کرنے کے سبب انسان دائمی عذاب میں مبتلا ہو گیا غرض رسول کی بعثت سے سوائے اس کے کوئی فائدہ نہیں ہوا کہ آدمی پر تنگی مسلط ہوگئی اور بندہ عذاب میں گرفتار ہو گیا پس رسول کی بعثت بلاء و مصیبت ہوگئی۔
متن المسلم : " واستدل اذا استوى الصدق والكذب في المقصود آثر العقل الصدق وفيه انه لا استواء فى نفس الامر لان لكل منهما لوازم وعوارض فهو تقدير مستحيل فيمتنع الايثار على ذالك التقدير "
ترجمہ : اور ( عقلی ہونے پر دلیل لائی گئی ہے کہ مقصود کے حاصل ہونے میں جب صدق و کذب دونوں برابر ہوتے ہیں تو عقل صدق کو ترجیح دیتی ہے۔ اور اس میں کلام یہ ہے کہ نفس الامر میں کذب وصدق مساوی نہیں ہیں اس لئے کہ ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کے لئے عوارض ولوازم
His statement: 'It does not harm...' — the author intends to respond to the Ash'ari response. The gist is that the Ash'ari response mentioned does not harm us Hanafis because we will now discuss the observation of public interest (maslahah), that observing public interest is intuitively good for both religious and non-religious people of intellect. If observing public interest were not rational but revealed, then non-religious deniers of revelation would never agree on its goodness. But since they all agree, it must be admitted that observing public interest is rational, and when some acts are proven rational, then the rationality of good and evil in all acts is proven. This is because there is consensus only on whether it is rational or revealed; no one holds a separation. Yes, the Ash'ari response would harm us if we claimed that good and evil are due to the essence of the act, for then it could be said that the goodness of beneficence and the evil of harm are not due to the essence, but they are due to observing public interest or its harm. However, we do not claim that they are due to the essence. Rather, our claim is that the goodness and badness of acts do not depend on the arrival of revelation. Now, whether their proof is from the essence, from an inseparable attribute, from a contingent attribute, or from observing public interest, the point is that the proof of good and evil is not from revelation, even if it is from something else, that is not problematic. So after this clarification, no doubt remains that the goodness of observing public interest and the evil of harming public interest are generally rational, not revealed. His statement: 'And he denied the consensus that it is the basis of the ruling' — The author mentions the Ash'ari denial of the Hanafi proof for the rationality of good and evil, and then responds to the denial. The denial: The Ash'aris object to the mentioned proof, saying that they do not accept that all rational people agree that good and evil necessitate the divine ruling. The author responds with his statement 'Lā yumanā': this denial does not affect our claim because we ourselves do not hold that good and evil necessitate the ruling, let alone claim consensus. Rather, we also hold that the ruling is from the beginning [established]. As for the mention of the proof, it only aims to establish the rationality of good and evil. So from this perspective, this denial does not even apply to those who hold that good and evil necessitate the ruling, because the proof that was objected to was not based on necessitation of the ruling but on the rationality of good and evil. Proof (2): Some great scholars have argued for the rationality of good and evil by saying that if good and evil were revealed, then prayer and fasting would have no difference [before revelation]; rather, in reality, both would be equal. So declaring one as obligatory and the other as prohibited would not be preferable [the one over the other] because they are equal; then a preference without a preponderant would be necessary — why would one be declared obligatory and the other prohibited? This also contradicts the wisdom of the Commander, who is absolutely wise. Proof (3): If good and evil were revealed, then sending a prophet would be a trial, tribulation, and calamity, not a mercy. Because before the prophet was sent, people were at ease and comfort, with no accountability for those things through which a person derives pleasure. But after the prophet's mission, due to committing some acts, a person falls into eternal punishment. In short, the prophet's mission brought no benefit except that hardship was imposed on man and he became trapped in punishment. So the prophet's mission became a calamity. The matn of Muslim: 'And it is argued: When truth and falsehood are equal in purpose, reason prefers truth. It is responded that there is no equality in reality, because each has its necessary concomitants and accidents. So this is an impossible supposition, and preference is impossible on that supposition.' Translation: And a proof is brought for rationality: When truth and falsehood are equal in achieving the purpose, reason prefers truth. And there is a discussion that in reality, truth and false are not equal because each has its attendant circumstances and consequences. So this estimation is impossible, and preference cannot be based on such an estimation.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul discussion defending the position that good (ḥusn) and evil (qubḥ) are rationally known, against the Ashʿari view that they are known only through revelation. The commentator begins by addressing the Ashʿari objection that appealing to public interest (maṣlaḥa) does not prove rationality. The Hanafi response is that even non-religious people agree that observing public interest is good, which proves it is a rational judgment. The commentator clarifies that Hanafis do not claim ḥusn and qubḥ are inherent in the essence of acts, but only that they are known independently of revelation. The second argument is that if ḥusn and qubḥ were merely revealed, then before revelation all acts would be equal, making it impossible for God to declare some obligatory and others prohibited without a preponderant, which violates divine wisdom. The third argument is that if morality were purely revelational, sending prophets would be a trial rather than a mercy, as they bring accountability and punishment. Finally, the matn from Muslim al-Thubut presents a rational argument that reason prefers truth over falsehood when they are equal in purpose, but the commentator notes that true equality is impossible in reality due to accompanying circumstances. This entire discussion is a fine example of Hanafi uṣūl using rational proofs (dalīl ʿaqlī) to establish a foundational principle.

Key Terms
حسن (ḥusn): Goodness, moral approbation; that which reason or revelation commends.
قبح (qubḥ): Evil, moral disapproval; that which reason or revelation condemns.
مصلحت عامہ (maṣlaḥat ʿāmma): Public interest; a rational good recognized universally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the Hanafi argument using the example of prayer and fasting before the mission of a prophet?
A) To prove that prayer is obligatory and fasting is prohibited.
B) To show that if values were only revealed, all acts would be equal and divine preference would be arbitrary.
C) To argue that reason cannot know any moral values.
D) To prove that prayer and fasting are both rationally good.

Key Takeaway
Hanafis prove the rationality of good and evil through universal consensus on public interest, the impossibility of arbitrary divine preference, and the purpose of prophethood—all without denying the necessity of revelation for detailed law.

Rational Good and Evil: Two Hanafi Proofs
Presenting two proofs that good/evil must be rational, not revealed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two rational proofs (dalil aqli) for the Hanafi position that good and evil are known by reason, and contrast it with the Ash'ari revealed-only position.

Core Idea
If good and evil were only from revelation, divine wisdom would lack a preponderator, and prophethood would be a calamity.

This passage presents two of the most powerful rational arguments (dalil aqli) used by the Hanafi school to establish that moral values are inherent in actions and known by reason, not solely by revelation. These proofs aim to show that without rational good and evil, the very concepts of divine wisdom and mercy would be undermined. As you work through them, you will learn how the Hanafi usul integrates reason alongside revelation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the claim: good and evil (husn wa qubh) are rationally knowable, not purely determined by revelation. This is the foundational Hanafi stance in usul al-fiqh.
This is opposed to the Ash'ari view that morality only comes from revelation.
2
Examine Proof (2): The Preponderator Argument
Proof 2 argues that if actions were morally neutral before revelation, then a wise commander would have no reason to prefer one action over another without a preponderating factor (murajjih). This would contradict divine wisdom (hikmah).
The principle 'tarjih bila murajjih muhal' (preference without a preponderator is impossible) is used.
3
Examine Proof (3): The Prophethood as Calamity Argument
Proof 3 argues that if morality were only from revelation, then before revelation people were at ease (no accountability), but after revelation they face eternal punishment for violations. Thus prophethood would be a calamity (bala') and affliction (fitnah), not a mercy (rahmah).
This contradicts the Quranic description of prophethood as mercy.
4
Consider the opposing Ash'ari view
The Ash'ari school holds that actions have no intrinsic moral value; God's command determines good and evil. Reason cannot independently judge morality.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf in usul.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school considers these proofs strong rational arguments. They conclude that rational good and evil are necessary to uphold divine wisdom and mercy, and this is the relied-upon position.
The tarjih is based on avoiding logical absurdities (muhal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل (۲): حسن وقیح کے عقلی ہونے پر بعض اعاظم نے دلیل دیتے ہوئے کہا ہے کہ حسن و قبح اگر شرعی ہوں تو نماز وز نا میں کوئی فرق نہیں رہ جائگا بلکہ نفس الامر میں دونوں ایک دوسرے کے مساوی ہو جائیں گے ( بعثت رسول سے پیشتر ) پس ان میں سے ایک کو واجب قرار دینا اور دوسرے کو حرام ٹھہرانا کوئی ان میں سے اپنی ضد سے اولی نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ دونوں مساوی ہیں پھر ترجیح بلا مرجح لازم آئے گا کہ مساوی ہونے کی صورت میں ایک کو واجب اور دوسرے کو حرام قرار کیوں دیا نیز حکمت آمر کے منافی ہے حالانکہ وہ حکیم مطلق قطعی ہے۔
دلیل (۳): حسن و فتح اگر شرعی ہوں تو رسول کی بعثتبلاء وفتنہ اور مصیبت ہوگی رحمت نہیں ہوگی کیونکہ بعثت رسول سے پیشتر لوگ آرام وسکون اور چین میں تھے کہ ان چیزوں میں مواخذہ نہیں تھا جنکے ذریعہ انسان لذت حاصل کرتا تھا لیکن رسول کی بعثت کے بعد بعض افعال کے ارتکاب کرنے کے سبب انسان دائمی عذاب میں مبتلا ہو گیا غرض رسول کی بعثت سے سوائے اس کے کوئی فائدہ نہیں ہوا کہ آدمی پر تنگی مسلط ہوگئی اور بندہ عذاب میں گرفتار ہو گیا پس رسول کی بعثت بلاء و مصیبت ہوگئی۔
Proof (2): Some eminent scholars have argued for the rationality of good and evil by saying: If good and evil were (only) revealed, then before the sending of the Messenger there would be no difference between prayer and fornication; rather, in reality both would be equal (before prophethood). Then to declare one of them obligatory and the other prohibited would be no better than their opposite, because they are equal. Then preference without a preponderant factor would necessarily follow. Why declare one obligatory and the other prohibited when they are equal? Also, it contradicts the wisdom of the Commander, while He is absolutely and decisively Wise.
Proof (3): If good and evil were (only) revealed, then the sending of the Messenger would be a calamity and an affliction, not a mercy. Because before the sending of the Messenger, people were at ease and comfort, as they were not taken to account for those things through which a person derives pleasure. But after the sending of the Messenger, due to committing certain actions, a person falls into eternal punishment. So the sending of the Messenger brought nothing but hardship and punishment for the servant, hence it became a calamity and affliction.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents two rational proofs (dalil aqli) from the Hanafi school to establish that good and evil (husn wa qubh) are known by reason, not solely by revelation. The first proof argues that if they were purely revealed, then before prophethood all actions would be equal, and a wise commander would not prefer one without a preponderant factor. The second proof argues that if good and evil were only from revelation, then the sending of prophets would be a punishment, not a mercy, because people were previously not accountable. These proofs are used to support the Mu'tazili-Hanafi position that reason can independently know moral values, contrary to the Ash'ari position that morality is entirely revealed.

Key Terms
حسن و قبح (husn wa qubh): Good and evil; moral value of actions as known by reason according to Hanafi usul.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Reason would be sufficient.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school demonstrates that rational good and evil are necessary to preserve divine wisdom and mercy, using two aqli proofs that refute the pure revelation-based view.

The Fourth Proof for Rational Good and Evil: Preference for Truth and Its Refutation
Presenting the fourth proof (preference of truth) and the objection that truth and falsehood are never equal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth proof for rational good and evil, state the objection based on the impossibility of perfect equality, and evaluate the strength of the proof in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Truth is inherently good, but the proof from universal preference is challenged because truth and falsehood are never truly equal.

This lesson examines the fourth proof for the rational recognition of good and evil, as presented in the Hanafi usul text Muslim al-Thubut. The proof argues that even when truth and falsehood equally achieve a worldly purpose, reason prefers truth, indicating truth's inherent goodness. However, the author raises an objection: truth and falsehood cannot be perfectly equal in reality, undermining the proof. We will analyze this objection and its implications for Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proof
Read the matn: when truth and falsehood are equal in achieving a purpose, reason gives preference to truth. This is taken as evidence that truth is inherently good (ḥasan li-dhātihi) and that reason can perceive this.
The proof is aqli (rational), not naqli (textual).
2
Identify the objection
The commentator objects that truth and falsehood can never be perfectly equal in reality because each has its own necessary concomitants (lawāzim) and accidental attributes (ʿawāriḍ). Thus the assumption of equality is impossible, and the proof collapses.
The objection uses the framework of lawazim and awarid.
3
Relate to the broader dispute
Connect this objection to the Ashʿarī position that good and evil are known only through revelation, not reason. The Hanafīs argue that reason does perceive inherent goodness/evil, but this proof is not decisive.
This is part of the ikhtilaf between Māturīdī and Ashʿarī theology.
4
Evaluate the strength of the proof
Consider whether the objection refutes the proof entirely. The Hanafīs maintain that the proof still shows a general rational inclination toward truth, even if the assumption of perfect equality is unrealistic.
The objection weakens but does not destroy the proof; it remains a supporting argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن المسلم : " واستدل اذا استوى الصدق والكذب في المقصود آثر العقل الصدق وفيه انه لا استواء فى نفس الامر لان لكل منهما لوازم وعوارض فهو تقدير مستحيل فيمتنع الايثار على ذالك التقدير "
ترجمہ : اور ( عقلی ہونے پر دلیل لائی گئی ہے کہ مقصود کے حصول میں جب صدق و کذب دونوں برابر ہوتے ہیں تو عقل صدق کو ترجیح دیتی ہے۔ اور اس میں کلام یہ ہے کہ نفس الامر میں کذب وصدق مساوی نہیں ہیں اس لئے کہ ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کے لئے عوارض ولوازم ہیں پس وہ امر تحصیل کو فرض کرنا ہے لہذا اس تقدیر پر ایثار متنع ومحال ہو گا ۔
تشریح: - قوله واستدل اذا استوى الصدق والكذب حسن و فتح کے سلسلے میں حنفیہ کے مذہب مختار پر یہاں سے دوسری دلیل ذکر کر رہے ہیں۔ اس دلیل میں مصنف کو اعتراض ہے اس لئے پہلے ہی صیغہ مجہول سے اس کے ضعف کی طرف اشارہ کیا۔ دلیل (۴):- کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ مقصد کے حاصل ہونے میں اگر یقین ہو کہ سچ بولنے پر اور جھوٹ بکنے پر بہر دوصورت مقصود و مطلوب حاصل ہو جائے گا یعنی صدق و کذب دونوں میں سے کسی ایک سے مطلوب حاصل ہو سکتا ہے کہ مقصود تک پہونچنے میں دونوں مساوی ہیں تو اسوقت عقل ، کذب کو لامحالہ چھوڑ کر محض صدق کو ترجیح دیتی ہے پس عقل اس جگہ صدق کو اسی لئے ترجیح دے رہی ہے کہ صدق فی ذاتہ حسن ہے کیونکہ اگر صدق کا حسن ذاتی و عقلی نہ ہوتا تو عقل صدق کو کذب پر ہرگز ترجیح نہ دیتی چنانچہ یہی وجہ ہے کہ وہ لوگ بھی صدق کو کذب پر ترجیح دیتے ہیں جو کسی دین کے پابند نہیں ہیں ۔ قوله وفيه انه لا استواء فى نفس الامر
اس عبارت سے دلیل مذکور کی تزییف مقصود ہے۔ حاصل اعتراض یہ ہے کہ صدق و کذب نفس الامر میں مساوی نہیں ہو سکتے کیونکہ صدق و کذب ہر ایک کے لئے ایسے لوازم و عوارض ہیں جو ایک دوسرے کے متغائر و متنافی ہیں چنانچہ صدق کے لوازم میں سے واقعہ کے مطابق ہونا اور عوارض میں سے تقاضائے مصلحت کے موافق ہونا ہے اور اس کے برعکس کذب کے لوازم میں سے واقعہ کے مطابق نہ ہونا اور عوارض میں سے تقاضائے مصلحت کے خلاف ہونا یعنی مفسدہ ہے لہذا دونوں ایک دوسرے کے جمیع حیثیات سے مساوی نہیں ہو سکتے ۔ اور جب صدق و کذب کا جمیع مقاصد میں جمیع جہات کے اعتبار سے مساوی ہونا محال ہے تو مستدل کا ان دونوں کو مساوی کہنا امر ستحیل کو مقدر ماننے کے قبیل سے ہو گا
اور جب مساوات محال تو اس تقدیر پر عقل کا صدق کو ترجیح دینا محال ہو جائے گا کہ محال مستلزم محال ہوتا ہے حاشیہ میں ہے۔
اى على تقدير وقوع المساوات لجواز استلزام المحال للمحال ومن
المع لوم أن عدم الايثار ليس بمحال بالنظر الى الاستواء فتدبر
[Matn of Muslim: "And it is argued that when truth and falsehood are equal in achieving the purpose, reason gives preference to truth. In this (argument) there is (the objection) that there is no equality in reality because each has its necessary concomitants and accidents, so it is an assumption of something impossible, and preference is prevented upon that assumption."
Translation: And (the proof for rationality is presented that) when in achieving the purpose truth and falsehood are both equal, reason gives preference to truth. And in this (argument) there is a discussion that in reality truth and falsehood are not equal, because each of them has accidents and necessary concomitants. So that assumption is impossible; therefore upon that assumption preference is prevented and impossible.
Commentary: His statement "and it is argued that when truth and falsehood are equal" — regarding good and evil, according to the chosen madhhab of the Hanafis, he is presenting a second proof here. In this proof, the author has an objection, so he indicated its weakness from the beginning by using the passive voice. The summary of the fourth proof is that if it is certain that by speaking truth and by speaking falsehood, in both cases the desired purpose will be achieved, i.e., the purpose can be attained by either truth or falsehood, so they are equal in reaching the purpose, then at that time reason inevitably leaves falsehood and prefers mere truth. So reason prefers truth here only because truth is good in itself, because if truth's goodness were not inherent and rational, reason would never prefer truth over falsehood. This is why even those who do not adhere to any religion prefer truth over falsehood. His statement "and in it there is no equality in reality" — this phrase is intended to weaken the mentioned proof. The summary of the objection is that truth and falsehood cannot be equal in reality because each has its own necessary concomitants and accidents that are mutually exclusive and contrary. Thus, the concomitants of truth include conformity to reality, and among its accidents is compatibility with the requirement of benefit. Conversely, the concomitants of falsehood include non-conformity to reality, and among its accidents is opposition to the requirement of benefit, i.e., harm. Therefore, they cannot be equal in all respects. And when equality of truth and falsehood in all purposes and in all aspects is impossible, then the arguer's claim that they are equal is like assuming an impossible thing. And when equality is impossible, then reason's preference of truth upon that assumption becomes impossible, because an impossible thing entails an impossible thing. In the gloss it says: meaning, upon the assumption of the occurrence of equality, it is permissible for the impossible to entail the impossible. And it is known that non-preference is not impossible with respect to equality, so reflect.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for rational good and evil, taken from the matn of Muslim al-Thubut. The proof argues that even when both truth and falsehood achieve the same worldly purpose, reason instinctively prefers truth, indicating that truth is inherently good (hasan li-dhatihi). This is evidenced by the fact that even non-religious people prefer truth. The author, however, raises an objection found in the matn: truth and falsehood are never truly equal in reality because each carries distinct necessary concomitants (lawazim) and accidental attributes (awarid). Truth conforms to reality and benefits, falsehood does not. Therefore, the assumption of equality is impossible, and the proof collapses. The commentary explains that the author signals doubt by using the passive voice. The gloss adds further nuance: if equality were assumed, non-preference would not necessarily be impossible.

Key Terms
صدق (sidq): Truthfulness; conformity of speech to reality.
کذب (kidhb): Falsehood; speech that does not conform to reality.
لوازم (lawazim): Necessary concomitants; inherent qualities that necessarily accompany something.
عوارض (awarid): Accidental attributes; non-essential qualities that may vary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say the objection weakens the fourth proof?
A) Because truth and falsehood are never truly equal in reality.
B) Because reason always prefers truth.
C) Because the proof relies on a divine command.
D) Because non-religious people prefer lies.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof for rational good and evil is intuitive but not decisive; the objection highlights the difficulty of assuming perfect equality, yet the proof remains a supportive argument in Hanafi usul.
